Faith and Disbelief
beginner
5 min
Sin and Innovation Do Not Remove Faith
General rule that sin and innovation do not remove faith, with exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ahl al-Sunna principle that sins and innovations (based on interpretation) do not nullify faith, except for two specific beliefs: anthropomorphism and denial of God's knowledge of particulars.

Core Idea
Faith remains with sin and innovation, only clear disbelief removes it.

After addressing a specific case, the text lays down a foundational creedal principle: transgression and disobedience do not erase faith, nor does innovation (bid'a) when it stems from misinterpretation. However, the author notes two exceptions that are unanimously considered kufr.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. This section summarizes the general rule and exceptions.
The passage starts with 'والحاصل' which signals a conclusion.
2
Identify the general rule about sin
The first part states that fisq and disobedience do not remove faith, and there is no intermediate state between faith and disbelief.
This refutes the position of the Khawarij and Mu'tazila who consider major sins as removing faith.
3
Understand the ruling on innovation
Innovation (bid'a) also does not remove faith, and the author gives examples from the Mu'tazila: denial of attributes, denial that God creates actions, denial of beatific vision. These are based on misinterpretation.
The phrase 'ولو كان على وجه الفساد' indicates the interpretation may be flawed, yet still not kufr.
4
Note the two exceptions
Two specific beliefs are excluded: tajsim (anthropomorphism) and denial of God's knowledge of particulars. These are kufr by consensus.
This shows that not all errors are equal; some are so foundational that they remove faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم في بسط الإمام الكلام على نفي تكفير أرباب الآثام من أهل القبلة ولو من أهل البدعة دلالة على أن سب الشيخين ليس بكفر كما صححه أبو الشكور السالمي في تمهيده، وذلك لعدم ثبوت مبناه وعدم تحقق معناه، فإن سب المسلم فسق كما في حديث ثابت، وحينئذ يستوي الشيخان وغيرهما في هذا الحكم؛ ولأنه لو فرض أن أحدا قتل الشيخين بل والختنين بوصف الجمع لا يخرج عن كونه مسلما عند أهل السنة والجماعة، ومن المعلوم أن السب دون القتل، نعم لو استحل السب أو القتل فهو كافر لا محالة وعلى تقدير ثبوت الحديث فيجب أن يؤوّل كما أوّل حديث : من ترك صلاةً متعمدا فقد كفر). (والحاصل أن الفسق والعصيان لا يزيل الإيمان فيصير كافرا ولا واسطة، وكذا البدعة لا تزيل الإيمان والمعرفة كإنكار المعتزلة صفات الله تعالى وخلق أفعال العباد وجواز رؤيته سبحانه في المعاد؛ لأنه مبني على تأويل ولو كان على وجه الفساد إلا التجسيم وإنكار علم الله بالجزئيات، فإنه يكفر بهما بالإجماع من غير نزاع.
Then in the Imam's expansion of speech on negating the excommunication of the sinful people of the qibla, even if they are from the people of innovation, it indicates that reviling the two shaykhs is not disbelief, as Abū al-Shukūr al-Sālimī corrected in his Tamhīd. That is because its basis is not established and its meaning is not verified, for reviling a Muslim is transgression (fisq) as is established in a sound hadith. Thus the two shaykhs and others are equal in this ruling; because even if one supposes that someone killed the two shaykhs—nay, even the two caliphs in a collective sense—he would not depart from being a Muslim according to Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jamā'a, and it is known that reviling is less than killing. Indeed, if he deemed reviling or killing lawful, then he is a disbeliever without doubt. And on the assumption that a hadith (regarding killing or reviling) is established, it must be interpreted just as the hadith 'Whoever intentionally leaves prayer has disbelieved' is interpreted. (The conclusion is that transgression and disobedience do not remove faith so that one becomes a disbeliever, nor is there an intermediate state. Likewise, innovation does not remove faith and knowledge, such as the Mu'tazila's denial of Allāh's attributes, His creation of acts of servants, and the permissibility of seeing Him in the Hereafter; because it is based on interpretation, even if it is erroneous. Except for anthropomorphism (tajsīm) and denial of Allāh's knowledge of particulars, for one becomes a disbeliever by these by consensus without dispute.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a proof that reviling the two shaykhs (Abū Bakr and 'Umar) does not constitute kufr. The commentator begins by citing an authority (Abū al-Shukūr al-Sālimī) and provides two arguments: first, the basis for declaring it kufr is not established; second, reviling a Muslim is only fisq (transgression) according to a hadith, and thus it applies equally to all, including the two shaykhs. To strengthen the point, he argues that even killing them would not remove one from Islam, so reviling (which is less) certainly does not. The only exception is if the reviler deems the act lawful (istiḥlāl). Then the commentator concludes with a general rule: sin and innovation do not remove faith, except for anthropomorphism (tajsīm) and denying God's knowledge of particulars, which are kufr by consensus. Note: The examples of Mu'tazila denials are given as cases of erroneous interpretation that do not necessitate takfīr.

Fisq and Bid'ah Do Not Remove Iman
Author states rule on fisq/bid'ah and lists exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that fisq and bid'ah do not remove iman, and you will be able to identify the two beliefs that are considered kufr by consensus regardless of interpretation.

Core Idea
Sin and innovation do not nullify faith; only specific rejections do.

This passage from al-Fiqh al-Akbar clarifies a key distinction: not every sin or innovation takes a person out of faith. The author states the general rule and then specifies two beliefs—tajsim (anthropomorphism) and denial of God's knowledge of particulars—that are unequivocally kufr. Let's read carefully to grasp both the rule and its exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the author's own wording come through.
Pay attention to the flow from the general rule to the exceptions.
2
Identify the general rule
Notice the claim: fisq and disobedience do not remove faith, and similarly bid'ah does not remove faith and knowledge. There is no intermediate state.
This is a clear statement against extremism in takfir.
3
Note the example of Mu'tazila
The author gives the Mu'tazila's denial of attributes, creation of actions, and vision of Allah as an example of kufr by consensus. Understand that these denials are considered to nullify iman despite being from a group that claims to be Muslim.
This shows that not all bid'ah is equal; some amounts to kufr.
4
Understand the exception clause
The phrase 'على تأويل ولو كان على وجه الفساد' indicates that even if the denial is based on a corrupt interpretation, it is not kufr—except for tajsim and denial of Allah's knowledge. These two are always kufr.
This is a nuanced position: interpretation can excuse, but not for these core issues.
5
Reflect on implications
Ask yourself how this passage guides us in judging others' faith. It warns against wholesale takfir of sinners or innovators, but identifies clear red lines.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أن الفسق والعصيان لا يزيل الإيمان فيصير كافرا ولا واسطة، وكذا البدعة لا تزيل الإيمان والمعرفة كإنكار المعتزلة صفات الله تعالى وخلق أفعال العباد وجواز رؤيته سبحانه في المعاد؛ فإنه يكفر بهما بالإجماع من غير نزاع. على تأويل ولو كان على وجه الفساد إلا التجسيم وإنكار علم الله تعالى، فإنه يكفر بهما بالإجماع من غير نزاع.
The summary is that fisq and disobedience do not remove faith to the point of becoming a disbeliever, and there is no intermediate state. Likewise, innovation (bid'ah) does not remove faith and knowledge, like the denial of the Mu'tazila of the attributes of Allah, the creation of the actions of servants, and the permissibility of seeing Him in the Hereafter; for that is kufr by consensus without dispute. On the basis of interpretation even if it is corrupt, except for tajsim (anthropomorphism) and denial of Allah's knowledge of particulars, for both are kufr by consensus without dispute.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal statement affirming that fisq (immorality) and disobedience do not negate faith (iman), nor does innovation (bid'ah) remove faith and knowledge. The author then gives an example: the Mu'tazila's denial of God's attributes, creation of human actions, and the possibility of seeing God in the afterlife—these are kufr by consensus. He then clarifies that even if these denials are based on a corrupt interpretation, they are still not kufr, except for two matters: tajsim (anthropomorphism) and denial of God's knowledge of particulars. These two are kufr by consensus without dispute. The passage thus draws a clear line between sinful deviations and outright disbelief.

Cursing Major Sinners and the Case of Yazeed
Qunawi's prohibition of cursing major sinners and author's comments on Yazeed and Qadariyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why cursing a major sinner is impermissible, how this applies to Yazeed, and why corrupt beliefs like those of the Qadariyya are not necessarily disbelief.

Core Idea
Faith remains despite major sins; cursing is forbidden and not all deviance is kufr.

This passage addresses the sensitive topic of cursing individuals guilty of major sins, specifically Yazeed ibn Muawiya. It also clarifies the theological boundary between major sin and disbelief, correcting those who equate corrupt beliefs with apostasy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the quotes from Qunawi and the author's responses.
2
Understand Qunawi's ruling
Identify Qunawi's core ruling: a major sinner should not be cursed because their faith remains intact.
This is based on the principle that faith does not decrease by sin.
3
See how the author applies the rule to Yazeed
Notice that the author explicitly applies this rule to Yazeed, stating his faith is established and cursing him is impermissible.
This is a direct application of the general rule to a specific historical figure.
4
Examine the dispute over the Abu Hanifa quote
Qunawi reports a statement from Abu Hanifa about not declaring Khawarij disbelievers, but the author questions its authenticity.
This shows the importance of textual reliability.
5
Analyze the Qadariyya discussion
Understand Qunawi's claim that calling something a 'sin' implies takfir, and the author's correction that Qadariyya beliefs are major sins, not kufr.
This clarifies that not all corrupt beliefs reach the level of disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال القونوي في "شرح عمدة النسفي": ولا يلعن صاحب الكبيرة؛ لأن إيمانه معه ولم ينقص بارتكابه الكبيرة والمؤمن لا يجوز لعنه انتهى. ولا يخفي أن إيمان يزيد محقق ولا يثبت كفره بدليل ظني فضلا عن دليل قطعي، لعنه فلا يجوز بخصوصه . وأما ما نقله القونوي حيث قال: قد ذكر أبو حنيفة رحمه الله في "الفقه الأكبر" أن أبا حنيفة رحمه الله سئل عن الخوارج المحكمة؟ فقال: هم أخبث الخوارج، فقيل: أنكفّرهم؟ فقال : لا . ولكن نقاتلهم على ما قاتلهم الأئمة من أهل الخير كعلي بن أبي طالب وعمر بن عبد العزيز» فما وجدناه في النسخ المصححة ولا في الأصول المعتبرة. ثم قال القونوي وفي قوله بذنب إشارة إلى تكفيره بفساد اعتقاده كفساد اعتقاد المجسمة والمشبهة والقدرية ونحوهم؛ لأن ذلك لا يسمى ذنبا والكلام في الذنب، انتهى. ولا يخفى أن اعتقاد القدرية لا يعد من الأمور الكفرية، بل يعد من كبائر الذنوب وأقبحها حيث لا توبة للمبتدع.
And al-Qunawi said in 'Sharh ʻUmdat al-Nasafi': A committer of a major sin should not be cursed, because his faith is with him and did not decrease by his commission of the major sin, and a believer is not permissible to curse. End quote. It is not hidden that Yazeed's faith is established and his disbelief is not proven by a probable evidence, let alone a definitive one, so cursing him especially is not permissible. As for what al-Qunawi reported, where he said: Abu Hanifa, may God have mercy on him, mentioned in 'al-Fiqh al-Akbar' that Abu Hanifa was asked about the Khawarij al-Muhakkima; he said: They are the worst of the Khawarij. It was said: Do we declare them disbelievers? He said: No, but we fight them as the imams of the good people fought them, like Ali ibn Abi Talib and Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz. But we did not find this in the corrected copies nor in the reliable sources. Then al-Qunawi said: And in his saying 'by a sin' there is an indication of declaring him a disbeliever due to corruption of his belief, like the corruption of belief of the Mujassima, Mushabbiha, Qadariyya, and similar groups; because that is not called a sin, and the discussion is about sin. End quote. And it is not hidden that the belief of the Qadariyya is not counted among the matters of disbelief, but rather is counted among the major sins and the ugliest of them, since there is no repentance for the innovator.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage combines al-Qunawi's statement from his commentary and the author's responses. Qunawi first rules that a committer of a major sin should not be cursed because his faith remains intact. The author affirms this and applies it to Yazeed, asserting that his faith is established and that cursing him specifically is impermissible. Qunawi then reports a supposed statement from Abu Hanifa about not declaring the Khawarij disbelievers, but the author notes that this report is not found in reliable copies. Qunawi further claims that calling a major sin 'a sin' indicates takfir for those with corrupt beliefs like the Mujassima, Mushabbiha, and Qadariyya, because those beliefs are not 'sins' in the usual sense. The author objects, arguing that the belief of the Qadariyya is not disbelief but a major sin, with the added severity that there is no repentance for the innovator.

The Major Sinner as a True Believer: Defining Iman
The author defines iman and defends calling a major sinner a true believer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ahl al-Sunna consider a major sinner a true believer, based on the definition of iman as affirmation and confession, not including actions.

Core Idea
Faith is in the heart and tongue; actions perfect it, but do not define it.

This passage tackles a central point of contention in early Islamic theology: does committing a major sin remove one from faith? The author presents the Sunni position that a major sinner remains a true believer (not merely metaphorically), grounded in the definition of iman itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the key terms and how the author defines iman.
The passage uses 'ḥaqīqatan' to emphasize literal truth.
2
Identify the definition of iman
The author states that iman is affirmation (taṣdīq) and confession (iqrār) with the tongue. Find this in the text.
Actions are not part of this definition.
3
Contrast the positions
Compare the Sunni view (actions are perfection) with the Khawarij and Mu'tazila views (actions as condition or part).
This contrast is the 'origin of disagreement'.
4
Apply the lesson
Understand that a major sinner is still called a believer truly, not metaphorically, because the essence of faith remains intact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالجنان (ونسميه) أي مرتكب الكبيرة (مؤمنا (حقيقة أي لا مجازا؛ لأن الإيمان هو التصديق والإقرار باللسان، وأما العمل بالأركان فهو من كمال الإيمان وجمال الإحسان عند أهل السنة والجماعة، وشرط أو شطر عند الخوارج والمعتزلة، فهذا منشأ الخلاف في المسألة
With the heart (al-janān), and we call him—that is, the committer of a major sin—a believer truly (ḥaqīqatan), i.e., not metaphorically, because faith (al-īmān) is affirmation (al-taṣdīq) and confession (al-iqrār) with the tongue. As for action (al-ʻamal) by the pillars, it is part of the perfection of faith and the beauty of excellence according to Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jamāʻa, and it is a condition or a part according to the Khawārij and the Muʻtazila. Thus this is the origin of the disagreement on the issue.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a definition and doctrinal clarification. The author states that the committer of a major sin is called a believer 'truly' (ḥaqīqatan), not metaphorically. The reason is that faith (īmān) is defined as affirmation (taṣdīq) in the heart and confession (iqrār) with the tongue. Actions (aʻmāl) according to the pillars of Islam are part of the perfection (kamāl) of faith, not its essence. This is the position of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jamāʻa, in contrast to the Khawārij who make action a condition (sharṭ) or part (shāṭr) of faith, and the Muʻtazila who hold an intermediate position. The author presents this as the origin of the disagreement on whether a major sinner remains a true believer.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A major sinner is only a believer metaphorically, or loses his faith entirely.
Correction: According to Ahl al-Sunna, a major sinner is a true believer (ḥaqīqatan) because faith is defined as affirmation and confession, not including actions.

D) Faith is affirmation and confession, actions are a part.

Key Takeaway
A major sinner remains a true believer because faith is defined by heart affirmation and tongue confession, not by actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Believing Sinner: Definition and Key Terms
The author defines and explains the possibility of being a believing sinner without being an unbeliever.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of a believing sinner (fāsiq) in classical Islamic theology and how sin does not negate belief.

Core Idea
A believer can be a sinner without becoming an unbeliever.

One of the most important distinctions in Islamic theology is between belief and sin. Many assume that committing major sins removes a person from the fold of faith. This lesson examines a key passage from the sharh that defines the believing sinner and clarifies why sinfulness and belief can coexist.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage uses three key terms: مؤمن, فاسق, كافر.
2
Identify the three elements of the definition
The passage defines the person as a believer by attestation and confession, a sinner by disobedience and persistence, and not an unbeliever due to steadfastness. Identify each element and how they relate.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: why does sin not make someone an unbeliever? The passage answers: because the core of faith (attestation and confession) remains intact. Consider how this shapes the Islamic understanding of faith and sin.
This connects to the broader theology of faith and works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجوز أن يكون مؤمنا فاسقا غير كافر (ويجوز أن يكون) أي الشخص (مؤمنا) أي بتصديقه وإقراره (فاسقا) أي بعصيانه وإصراره غير كافر أي لثباته في مقام اعتباره.
And it is permissible that (a person) be a believer (by his attestation and confession), a sinner (by his disobedience and persistence), not an unbeliever because of his steadfastness in the station of his consideration.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage provides a definition and explanation of the concept of a 'believing sinner' (fāsiq). The author begins with the statement 'ويجوز أن يكون مؤمنا فاسقا غير كافر' (it is permissible that [a person] be a believer, a sinner, not an unbeliever). Then he unpacks each term: 'مؤمنا' (believer) means by his attestation (taṣdīq) and confession (iqrār) of faith; 'فاسقا' (sinner) means by his disobedience (ʿiṣyān) and persistence (iṣrār) in sin; and 'غير كافر' (not an unbeliever) means because of his steadfastness (thubāt) in the station of being considered a believer (iʿtibār). In other words, sin does not negate the fundamental status of belief as long as the person maintains the core acts of faith—attestation and confession—even if he commits major sins.

Key Terms
مؤمن
فاسق
كافر
تصديق
إقرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A person who commits major sins becomes an unbeliever.
Correction: The passage clarifies that a believer remains a believer despite sin, as long as attestation and confession are maintained.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what makes a person a believer despite being a sinner?
A) His repentance from sin
B) His attestation and confession of faith
C) His avoidance of all major sins
D) His membership in a Muslim community

Key Takeaway
A person remains a believer as long as they maintain attestation and confession of faith, even if they commit sins.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you currently persisting in any sin that you know is wrong, yet you still consider yourself a believer? How does this lesson's distinction between being a believer and being a sinner challenge you to repent without losing hope in God's mercy?
?
If you were to die tonight in your current state of sin, would you be certain that you are still a believer? What evidence from your own attestation and confession gives you that assurance?
?
Knowing that your sin does not make you an unbeliever, do you find yourself taking sin lightly? Or does this truth drive you to deeper gratitude and determination to leave sin behind?

Abu Hanifa and the Murji'a Label
Al-Qunawi's report on Abu Hanifa's Murji' label and his statement on hope and fear.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Abu Hanifa was called Murji' and how his view differed from the heretical Murji'a doctrine.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa's 'irja' meant postponing judgment, not denying sin's harm.

The term 'Murji'a' is often misunderstood. This lesson unpacks a report from al-Qunawi that explains why Abu Hanifa was labeled a Murji' and what he actually said about sinners. It sets the record straight on a key historical confusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage from al-Qunawi
Read the Arabic passage that cites al-Qunawi's report. Notice the reason given for calling Abu Hanifa a Murji' and his own words on hope and fear.
Al-Qunawi is the source for this attribution.
2
Understand 'irja' as postponement
The term 'irja' means postponement. Abu Hanifa postponed the judgment of grave sinners to Allah's will, which is different from the Murji'a belief that sins do not affect faith.
3
Contrast with heretical Murji'a
Compare Abu Hanifa's position with the later blameworthy Murji'a who said 'sin does not harm with faith.' This highlights the difference.
This prepares for the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن القونوي ذكر أن أبا حنيفة رحمه الله كان يسمى مرجئا لتأخيره أمر صاحب الكبيرة إلى مشيئة الله تعالى والإرجاء التأخير، وكان يقول: إني لأرجو لصاحب الذنب الكبير والصغير وأخاف عليهما، وأنا أرجو لصاحب الذنب الصغير أخاف على صاحب الذنب الكبير، انتهى. وأما ما وقع في "الغنية" للشيخ عبد القادر الجيلاني رضي الله عنه عند ذكر الفرق الغير الناجية حيث قال: «ومنهم القدرية، وذكر أصنافا منهم، ثم قال ومنهم الحنفية وهم أصحاب أبي حنيفة نعمان بن ثابت رحمه الله، زعم أن الإيمان هو المعرفة والإقرار بالله ورسوله، وبما جاء من جملة على ما ذكر البرهوني في كتاب "الشجرة" عنده وهو اعتقاد فاسد وقول كاسد مخالف لاعتقاده في الفقه الأكبر»، وما نقله أصحابه أنه يقول: الإيمان هو مجرد التصديق دون الإقرار فإنه شرط عنده لإجراء أحكام الإسلام، ومناقض لسائر كتب العقائد الموضوعة للخلاف بين أهل السنة والجماعة وبين المعتزلة وأهل البدعة مع أن الإيمان هو المعرفة والإقرار هو المذهب المختار، بل هو أولى من أن يقال: الإيمان هو التصديق، والإقرار لأن التصديق الناشئ عن التقليد دون التحقيق مختلف في قبوله، بخلاف المعرفة الناشئة عن الدلالة مع الإقرار وبالإقرار فإنه إيمان بالإجماع، وأما الاكتفاء بالمعرفة دون الإقرار وبالإقرار دون المعرفة فهو في محل النزاع كما قاله بعض أهل الابتداع. ثم المرجئة المذمومة من المبتدعة ليسوا من القدرية، بل هم طائفة قالوا: لا يضر مع الإيمان
Know that al-Qūnawī mentioned that Abū Ḥanīfa, may Allah have mercy on him, was called Murji' because he postponed the matter of the committer of a major sin to Allah's will, and al-ijā' means postponement. He used to say: 'I indeed hope for the owner of the major and minor sin and fear for them both, and I hope for the owner of the minor sin and fear for the owner of the major sin.' End. As for what occurs in 'al-Ghunya' of Shaykh 'Abd al-Qādir al-Jīlānī, may Allah be pleased with him, when mentioning the non-saved sects where he said: 'Among them are the Qadariyya… and he mentioned types from them, then he said: Among them are the Ḥanafiyya, they are the followers of Abū Ḥanīfa Nu'mān ibn Thābit, may Allah have mercy on him, claiming that faith is knowledge (ma'rifa) and confession (iqrār) of Allah and His Messenger and of what came altogether, as al-Burhūnī mentioned in the book 'al-Shajara' with him, and it is a corrupt belief and a rotten statement contrary to his belief in al-Fiqh al-Akbar.' And what his followers transmitted that he says: 'Faith is mere attestation (taṣdīq) without confession, for it is a condition with him for implementing the rulings of Islam,' and it contradicts all the books of creed composed for the disagreement between Ahl al-Sunna wal-Jamā'a and the Mu'tazila and the people of innovation, while faith is knowledge and confession is the chosen doctrine. Rather it is more worthy than saying: faith is attestation and confession, because attestation arising from imitation (taqlīd) without verification is disputed in its acceptance, unlike knowledge arising from evidence (dalāla) with confession and by confession, for it is faith by consensus. As for sufficing with knowledge without confession, and with confession without knowledge, it is a matter of dispute, as some people of innovation have said. Then the blameworthy Murji'a from the innovators are not from the Qadariyya, rather they are a group who said: Sin does not harm with [faith]...
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a discussion about the Murji'a, Abū Ḥanīfa's alleged affiliation, and the correct definition of faith. The author begins by citing al-Qūnawī, who says Abū Ḥanīfa was called Murji' because he postponed (irjā') judgment on grave sinners to God's will. The term 'irjā' means postponement. Abū Ḥanīfa would say he hopes for both the great and small sinner but also fears for them—he hopes for the small sinner and fears for the great sinner. This clarifies that his usage of 'irjā' is theological hope, not the heretical Murji'a doctrine that sins do not harm faith. Next, the author addresses a problematic passage from 'al-Ghunya' by Shaykh 'Abd al-Qādir al-Jīlānī, where he lists the non-saved sects and includes the Ḥanafiyya (followers of Abū Ḥanīfa), attributing to them the belief that faith is knowledge and confession, and then claiming that this is contrary to Abū Ḥanīfa's actual view in al-Fiqh al-Akbar. The author rejects this, stating that Abū Ḥanīfa's own followers report that he said faith is mere attestation without confession—but that confession is a condition for applying Islamic rulings. The author argues that the correct view (al-madhhab al-mukhtār) is that faith is knowledge (ma'rifa) and confession (iqrār), because attestation based on imitation (taqlīd) without verification is disputed, whereas knowledge derived from evidence with confession is accepted by consensus. Sufficing with knowledge without confession or confession without knowledge is a matter of dispute, held by some innovators. Finally, the author clarifies that the blameworthy Murji'a are not the Qadariyya (who held a different error), but a group who say 'sin does not harm with faith'—a reference to the extreme Murji'a who believe that sins do not affect faith at all. The passage ends abruptly due to the page break.

Key Terms
الإرجاء
المرجئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abū Ḥanīfa was a Murji' in the heretical sense that sins do not harm faith.
Correction: Abū Ḥanīfa was called Murji' only because he postponed judgment to Allah; his own statements show he feared for sinners, contradicting the heretical Murji'a doctrine.

Checkpoint
Why was Abū Ḥanīfa called Murji' according to al-Qūnawī?
A) He believed sins do not harm faith.
B) He postponed the judgment of grave sinners to Allah's will.
C) He denied the existence of major sins.
D) He rejected the concept of sin altogether.

Key Takeaway
Abū Ḥanīfa's 'irjā' was about postponing judgment, not denying sin's impact on faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear someone described with a label like 'Murji'' in an accusatory way, do you immediately judge them without investigating what they actually believed? How can you apply Abu Hanifa's own approach of postponing judgment to your attitude towards those you disagree with?
?
Abu Hanifa said he both hopes and fears for sinners, including himself. Which do you lean toward more in your own heart—hope that Allah will forgive your major sins, or fear that you have not done enough? Are you unbalanced?
?
If you were accused of a theological error like Abu Hanifa was, would your response be as measured and precise as his? What does your reaction to criticism reveal about your reliance on Allah's will versus your own defense?

Faith: Knowledge, Confession, and Refuting al-Ghunya
Refutation of al-Ghunya's misattribution and affirmation of the correct definition of faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct definition of faith as knowledge (ma'rifa) and confession (iqrār) based on evidence, and how the author refutes a misattribution from al-Ghunya.

Core Idea
Faith is knowledge from evidence with confession, not mere imitation.

The author now addresses a problematic passage from 'al-Ghunya' that falsely attributes to Abū Ḥanīfa's followers a definition of faith that contradicts his own works. He then establishes the sound position that faith is knowledge supported by evidence and verbal confession, rejecting reliance on blind imitation (taqlīd).

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of al-Ghunya
Read the passage beginning with 'As for what occurs in al-Ghunya...' Note the author's strong rejection of the attribution to Abū Ḥanīfa.
The author calls it a 'corrupt belief and rotten statement'.
2
Identify the correct definition of faith
The author states that the chosen doctrine (al-madhhab al-mukhtār) is that faith is knowledge (ma'rifa) and confession (iqrār). Understand why this is superior to mere attestation (taṣdīq) based on imitation.
Knowledge from evidence is accepted by consensus.
3
Understand the blameworthy Murji'a
Finally, the author distinguishes the blameworthy Murji'a who say ‘sin does not harm with faith.’ This is the extreme view, not Abū Ḥanīfa's.
The passage ends abruptly, but the point is clear.

The Ahl al-Sunnah Position on Sinful Believers
Commentator enumerates three negations of Ahl al-Sunnah belief on sinful believers, then affirms Allah's will as decisive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ahl al-Sunnah middle position on the fate of sinful believers, rejecting both Murji'ite immunity and Mu'tazilite obligatory eternal punishment.

Core Idea
The fate of sinful believers rests solely in Allah's will, not in human certainty.

The early Muslim community debated the status of believers who commit major sins. Extreme views arose: some claimed faith alone guarantees paradise, others insisted on eternal punishment. This passage from Fiqh al-Akbar commentary articulates the orthodox Ahl al-Sunnah position, grounding it in Qur'anic authority while refuting both extremes. Let us examine the precise statements and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the three negations and the positive statement. Let the author's own words set the framework before analyzing.
2
Identify the rejected views
Notice which groups (Murji'ah, Mu'tazila) are refuted and why their positions are problematic. The commentator clarifies what Ahl al-Sunnah does not say.
The three negations target different extremes.
3
Understand the proof
The Quranic verse 4:48 is cited as the foundation. Reflect on how it supports the Ahl al-Sunnah stance: Allah forgives whom He wills, and repentance is accepted.
This refutes both over-optimism and over-pessimism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا نقول: إن المؤمن لا تضره الذنوب، وإنه لا يدخل النار ولا إنه يُخلّد فيها وإن كان فاسقا بعد أن يخرج من الدنيا مؤمنا. ومن (ولا نقول) أي بحسب الاعتقاد إن المؤمن لا تضره الذنوب) أي ارتكاب المعصية بعد حصول الإيمان والمعرفة (وإنه) أي المؤمن المذنب (لا) يدخل النار) كما يقوله المرجئة والملاحدة والإباحية ولا) (إنه أي ولا نقول إن المؤمن المذنب (يُخلَّد فيها وإن كان فاسقا) أي بارتكاب الكبائر جميعها بعد أن يخرج من الدنيا (مؤمنا أي مقرونا بحسن الخاتمة خلافا لما يقوله المعتزلة، وذلك لأن صاحب المعصية تحت المشيئة عند أهل السنة والجماعة لقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَن يُشْرَكَ بِهِ وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ لِمَنْ يَشَاءُ ﴾ [النساء:٤٨] من غير توبة، وإلا فهو سبحانه يقبل التوبة عن عباده ويغفر بها الشرك وغيره بمقتضى وعده وإخباره خلافا للمعتزلة حيث يقولون: يجب على الله تعالى عقاب العاصي وثواب المطيع وقبول التوبة وأمثالها.
And we do not say: Indeed the believer's sins do not harm him, and indeed he will not enter the Fire, nor indeed he will be eternally [punished] therein even if he is a sinner after he leaves the world as a believer. And from (we do not say) that is, according to belief, indeed the believer's sins do not harm him, that is, committing disobedience after attaining faith and knowledge. (and indeed he) that is, the sinful believer (does not) enter the Fire, as the Murji'ah, the atheists, and the libertines say, nor (indeed he, that is, we do not say that the sinful believer) will be eternally [punished] therein even if he is a sinner, that is, by committing all major sins after he leaves the world (as a believer, that is, accompanied by a good end), contrary to what the Mu'tazila say. And that is because the person of sin is under the will (mashī'ah) according to Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jamā'ah, due to His statement, exalted: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive association with Him, but He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' [al-Nisā' 4:48] without repentance. Otherwise, He, exalted, accepts repentance from His servants and forgives through it shirk and other things by virtue of His promise and informtion, contrary to the Mu'tazila where they say: It is obligatory upon Allah, exalted, to punish the disobedient and reward the obedient and accept repentance and the like.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a creedal statement and its commentary. It begins by stating what Ahl al-Sunnah does not say: we do not assert that a believer's sins are harmless, nor that he will definitely not enter the Fire, nor that he will be eternally punished in it, as long as he dies as a believer, even if he is a sinner. The commentary then unpacks each phrase. 'We do not say' means in terms of belief. The phrase 'that a believer's sins do not harm him' refers to committing disobedience after faith and knowledge—this is the view of the Murji'ah, who claim that faith alone suffices. Then 'that he will not enter the Fire' is the claim of the Murji'ah, atheists, and libertines, who believe no believer will ever enter Hell. Then 'that he will be eternally punished' even if he is a sinner (i.e., committing major sins) after dying as a believer—this contradicts the Mu'tazila, who hold that a sinner dying unrepentant will be eternally punished. The commentary explains that, according to Ahl al-Sunnah, the fate of the sinner is subject to Allah's will, based on the Quranic verse 4:48: Allah does not forgive shirk, but forgives lesser sins for whom He wills, even without repentance. If the sinner repents, Allah forgives even shirk by His promise. This refutes the Mu'tazila who assert that it is obligatory upon Allah to punish the disobedient and reward the obedient. Thus, the passage affirms Allah's absolute sovereignty and mercy while rejecting extreme views.

Key Terms
المؤمن
الذنوب
النار
الخلود
الفاسق
المشيئة
التوبة
المعتزلة
المرجئة
أهل السنة والجماعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ahl al-Sunnah denies any consequence for sins if one has faith.
Correction: Ahl al-Sunnah affirms that sins may lead to punishment, but the ultimate fate is under Allah's will, not automatically forgiven nor automatically punished eternally.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the Ahl al-Sunnah position regarding a believer who commits major sins and dies without repenting?
A) He will definitely be eternally punished in Hell.
B) He will never enter Hell because of his faith.
C) His fate is subject to Allah's will; he may be forgiven or punished.
D) He will enter Paradise immediately without any punishment.

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunnah affirms that sinful believers are subject to Allah's will; they may be forgiven or punished, contrary to both Murji'ite and Mu'tazilite extremes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your fate regarding your sins is entirely in Allah's hands, or do you secretly think you have earned forgiveness through your own efforts? How does this lesson expose that assumption? Will you repent now and rely on His mercy alone?
?
When you sin, do you fall into despair (like the Mu'tazila think) or complacency (like the Murji'ah)? Which extreme do you drift toward? What would it mean for you to live with the balance of hope and fear taught by Ahl al-Sunnah?
?
If you knew that Allah could forgive you without repentance, would that make you sin more? Or would it make you love Him more? Be honest: which reaction is closer to your heart? How can you change that today?

Critiquing al-Taftazani on Forgiveness and Repentance
Author critiques al-Taftazani's statement on forgiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author considers al-Taftazani's inclusion of 'with repentance' a slip and how the real point of disagreement with the Mu'tazila is forgiveness without repentance.

Core Idea
Repentance guarantees forgiveness; the real dispute is about forgiveness without it.

This lesson examines a specific criticism of al-Taftazani's commentary on a key Qur'anic verse about forgiveness. The author points out a subtle error that blurs the lines of theological disagreement. By clarifying this, we see how careful scholars must be with wording.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage plus its translation once to get the gist. Note that the author is critiquing a specific scholar's statement.
Look for the phrase 'سهو قلم' (slip of the pen) – that's the key criticism.
2
Understand the two criticisms
The author says al-Taftazani's statement is wrong in two ways. Identify both reasons. First, because it contradicts both groups; second, because with repentance there is no dispute at all.
3
Connect to the verse and hadith
The author cites a hadith and another verse to prove that repentance brings forgiveness. Ask yourself: how does this support the criticism?
4
Apply the distinction
Now you see the real issue: the verse 'He forgives what is less than that' refers to forgiveness without repentance – that is where the Mu'tazila disagree. With repentance, everyone agrees.
This clarifies the true point of contention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قول التفتازاني رحمه الله في "شرح العقائد عند قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ لِمَنْ يَشَاءُ [النساء:٤٨] من الصغائر والكبائر مع التوبة أو بدونها خلافا للمعتزلة، ففيه أن قوله مع التوبة سهو قلم ليس في محله من جهتين حيث خالف الطائفتين؛ لأن المشيئة بدون التوبة محل خلاف للمعتزلة، وأما معها فلا خلاف في المسألة كما صرح في "شرح المقاصد"، بأنهم أجمعوا على أن لا عذاب على التائب كما صح في الحديث : التائب من الذنب كمن لا ذنب له)) . () وكقوله تعالى: وَهُوَ الَّذِي يَقْبَلُ التَّوْبَةَ عَنْ عِبَادِهِ ﴾ [الشورى: ٢٥].
ثم لا نزاع في أن من المعاصي ما جعله الشارع أمارة التكذيب وعلم كونه كذلك بالأدلة الشرعية كالسجود للصنم، وإلقاء المصحف في القاذورات والتلفظ بكلمة الكفر ونحو ذلك مما ثبت بالأدلة أنه كفر، وبهذا يندفع ما يقال : إن الإيمان إذا كان عبارة عن التصديق والإقرار فينبغي أن لا يصير المقر باللسان المصدق بالجنان كافرا بشيء من أفعال الكفر وألفاظه ما لم يتحقق منه التكذيب أو الشك.
وأما احتجاج المعتزلة بأن الأمة بعد اتفاقهم على أن مرتكب الكبيرة فاسق اختلفوا في أنه مؤمن وهو مذهب أهل السنة والجماعة أو كافر وهو قول الخوارج، أو منافق وهو قول الحسن البصري رحمه الله فأخذنا بالمتفق عليه وتركنا المختلف فيه، وقلنا هو فاسق ليس بمؤمن ولا كافر ولا منافق، فمدفوع بأن هذا إحداث للقول المخالف لما أجمع عليه السلف من عدم المنزلة بين المنزلتين، فيكون باطلا على أن الحسن البصري رحمه الله رجع عنه آخرا كما صرح به في "البداية". والحاصل أن المعتزلة والخوارج خوارج عما انعقد عليه الإجماع فلا اعتداد بهم.
And as for the saying of al-Taftazani, may Allah have mercy on him, in 'Sharh al-'Aqā'id' regarding His statement, exalted: 'And He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' [al-Nisā' 4:48] – from minor and major sins with repentance or without it, contrary to the Mu'tazila, then in it [there is criticism]: his saying 'with repentance' is a slip of the pen, not in its place from two aspects, since he contradicted both groups [the Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunnah]; because the will without repentance is a matter of dispute with the Mu'tazila, but with it there is no dispute in the matter, as clearly stated in 'Sharh al-Maqāsid', that they are agreed that there is no punishment for the repentant person, as it is established in the hadith: 'The repentant from sin is like one who has no sin.' And like His statement, exalted: 'And He is the One who accepts repentance from His servants' [al-Shūrā 42:25]. Then there is no dispute that among sins are those which the Lawgiver has made a sign of denial [takdhīb] and it is known to be so by legal proofs, like prostrating to an idol, throwing the mushaf into filthy places, and uttering a word of disbelief and the like, which are established by proofs to be disbelief. And by this is refuted what is said: 'If faith is affirmation and acknowledgment, then it should not be that the one who affirms with the tongue and believes in the heart becomes a disbeliever by any act of disbelief or words unless there occurs from him denial or doubt.' And as for the argument of the Mu'tazila that the community, after agreement that the perpetrator of a major sin is a fāsiq, differed about whether he is a believer – which is the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jamā'ah – or a disbeliever – which is the saying of the Khawārij – or a hypocrite – which is the saying of al-Hasan al-Basri, may Allah have mercy on him; so they [the Mu'tazila] said: 'We take what is agreed upon and leave what is differed upon, and we say he is a fāsiq, neither a believer nor a disbeliever nor a hypocrite' – then it is refuted because this is an innovation of a saying contrary to what the salaf unanimously agreed upon: the negation of the intermediate status between the two statuses; thus it is false, especially since al-Hasan al-Basri, may Allah have mercy on him, later retracted it, as stated in 'al-Bidāyah'. The conclusion is that the Mu'tazila and Khawārij are outside what consensus has been established upon, so they are not to be counted.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part critical argument and refutation. It first addresses a statement from al-Taftazani in his commentary on the Creed, where he interpreted the verse 'He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' as covering minor and major sins whether with or without repentance, contrary to the Mu'tazila. The author points out that al-Taftazani's inclusion of 'with repentance' is a slip of the pen for two reasons: first, it contradicts both groups because the Mu'tazila agree that with repentance there is no punishment, so there is no disagreement; second, it implies that the verse's scope includes forgiveness without repentance, which is the actual point of dispute with the Mu'tazila. The author supports this by citing the hadith 'The repentant from sin is like one who has no sin' and the verse about accepting repentance. 

Next, the author asserts that there is no dispute that some sinful acts are considered by the Lawgiver as indicators of denial (takdhīb), such as prostrating to an idol, throwing the Qur'an into filthy places, or uttering words of disbelief. These are established as disbelief by legal proofs. This refutes the claim that if faith is merely affirmation and acknowledgment, then a person cannot become a disbeliever by outward acts or words unless there is actual denial or doubt. In other words, certain actions themselves constitute disbelief regardless of inner state.

Finally, the passage refutes the Mu'tazila's argument for an intermediate status (al-manzilah bayna al-manzilatayn). The Mu'tazila argued that since the community agrees that a major sinner is a fāsiq, and differed about whether he is a believer (Ahl al-Sunnah), a disbeliever (Khawārij), or a hypocrite (al-Hasan al-Basri), they take the agreed-upon term 'fāsiq' and leave the disputed terms, concluding that a fāsiq is neither a believer nor a disbeliever nor a hypocrite. The author refutes this by stating that it is an innovation (ihdāth) contradicting the consensus of the salaf that there is no intermediate status between faith and disbelief. Moreover, al-Hasan al-Basri himself later retracted this view. Therefore, the Mu'tazila and Khawārij are outside the consensus and are not to be considered.

Key Terms
التفتازاني
المشيئة
التوبة
شرح العقائد
شرح المقاصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the verse's forgiveness applies equally whether one repents or not, and that the dispute with the Mu'tazila covers both cases.
Correction: The actual dispute concerns forgiveness without repentance; with repentance, there is no disagreement that forgiveness occurs.

Checkpoint
What does the author identify as the real point of disagreement between Ahl al-Sunnah and the Mu'tazila regarding the verse?
A) Whether repentance is necessary for forgiveness of minor sins.
B) Whether forgiveness can occur without repentance.
C) Whether major sins can be forgiven at all.
D) Whether repentance must be public.

Key Takeaway
The real theological dispute about forgiveness is only about forgiveness without repentance; with repentance, all agree it is granted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author corrects a great scholar's slip. When your own understanding of Allah's mercy is imprecise—like thinking repentance isn't required or that some sins are too big—do you humbly correct yourself, or do you let it slide? What step will you take today to ensure your belief about forgiveness aligns with the Qur'an and consensus?
?
Al-Taftazani's error blurred a crucial distinction. In your heart, do you truly rely on repentance for forgiveness, or do you sometimes assume Allah will forgive without your turning back? What specific sin are you avoiding repenting from right now, and what is holding you back?
?
The lesson shows that even scholars can misrepresent two groups at once. Have you ever spoken carelessly about issues of faith, causing confusion? Commit right now to one concrete change in how you speak about Allah's attributes or matters of dispute, based on this lesson.

Refuting the Mu'tazila on Actions and Intermediate Status
Author refutes claims about actions causing disbelief and Mu'tazila's intermediate status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how certain actions are inherently signs of disbelief and why the Mu'tazila's argument for an intermediate status between faith and disbelief is invalid.

Core Idea
Some actions are themselves disbelief; there is no middle ground between faith and disbelief.

This lesson addresses two interconnected issues: first, that certain outward acts are legally considered signs of disbelief regardless of inner state; second, the Mu'tazila's claim that a major sinner occupies an intermediate status between faith and disbelief. The author refutes both using consensus and evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read carefully. Notice the shift from discussing al-Taftazani to a new point about acts that indicate disbelief.
The key term here is 'أمارة التكذيب' (sign of denial).
2
Understand the principle: actions as signs
The author says some sins are legally established as signs of denial (takdhīb), such as prostrating to an idol. These actions themselves constitute disbelief, regardless of inner conviction.
3
Refute the counterargument
The passage then refutes the idea that if faith is inner affirmation, then outward actions cannot make one a disbeliever. The author says certain actions are themselves proofs of disbelief.
This refutes a potential objection.
4
Examine the Mu'tazila's argument for intermediate status
The Mu'tazila argued that since the community agrees a major sinner is fāsiq but differs on further classification, we take the agreed term and leave the rest, resulting in a category between believer and disbeliever.
5
Understand the refutation
The author rejects this, saying it contradicts the salaf's consensus that there is no intermediate status. Also, al-Hasan al-Basri retracted his view.
Thus the Mu'tazila and Khawārij are outside the consensus.

Faith and Disbelief
beginner
4 min
Actions as Signs of Disbelief
Refuting the claim that outward acts cannot indicate disbelief without inner denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how certain outward acts are legally designated as signs of disbelief, and why this refutes the claim that inner affirmation alone prevents one from becoming a disbeliever.

Core Idea
The Lawgiver's designation of an act as a sign of denial makes it kufr.

In this passage, the author addresses a potential objection against the Sunni view of faith. The objection states that if faith is inner affirmation and verbal confession, then no outward act should be able to turn a person into a disbeliever. The response is that the Lawgiver has designated certain acts as signs of denial (amarat al-takdhib), and this designation overrides any assumption about inner state.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the premise and examples
The author states that there is no dispute that some sins are made signs of denial by the Lawgiver. Note the examples: prostration to an idol, throwing the Quran into impurities, uttering a word of disbelief.
These examples are not exhaustive but are given as clear cases.
3
Understand the refutation
The author uses this premise to refute the objection that one cannot become a disbeliever through actions alone if they inwardly affirm. The key point is that the Lawgiver's designation makes the act itself evidence of kufr.
The objection is rebutted by appealing to legal proofs, not rational speculation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لا نزاع في أن من المعاصي ما جعله الشارع أمارة التكذيب وعلم كونه كذلك بالأدلة الشرعية كالسجود للصنم، وإلقاء المصحف في القاذورات والتلفظ بكلمة الكفر ونحو ذلك مما ثبت بالأدلة أنه كفر، وبهذا يندفع ما يقال : إن الإيمان إذا كان عبارة عن التصديق والإقرار فينبغي أن لا يصير المقر باللسان المصدق بالجنان كافرا بشيء من أفعال الكفر وألفاظه ما لم يتحقق منه التكذيب أو الشك.
Then there is no dispute that among sins are those which the Lawgiver has made a sign of denial (amarat al-takdhib) and it is known to be so by legal proofs, such as prostration to an idol, throwing the Quran into impurities, uttering a word of disbelief, and similar things that are proven by evidence to be disbelief. And by this is repelled what is said: If faith is the affirmation (tasdiq) and verbal confession (iqrar), then one who confesses with the tongue and affirms with the heart should not become a disbeliever by any action or word of disbelief as long as actual denial (takdhib) or doubt (shakk) is not verified from him.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection against the Sunni conception of faith. It first states a premise: there is no dispute that some sins are designated by the Lawgiver (i.e., God and His Prophet) as signs of denial (amarat al-takdhib). Examples include prostrating to an idol, throwing the Quran into filth, or uttering a word of disbelief. These are known to be signs of kufr through textual evidence. Then the passage uses this premise to refute a counterargument: if faith is nothing but inner affirmation and verbal confession, then a person who outwardly confesses and inwardly affirms should not become a disbeliever by any outward action or word unless actual denial or doubt is proven. The refutation is that the Lawgiver's designation of certain acts as signs of denial is sufficient to render the doer a disbeliever, regardless of inner state, because such acts are inherently indicative of denial.

Key Terms
أمارة التكذيب (sign of denial)
إيمان (faith)
تصديق (affirmation)
إقرار (confession)
كفر (disbelief)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that a person can commit any outward act of disbelief without becoming a kafir as long as their heart affirms faith.
Correction: The passage clarifies that certain acts are legally defined by the Lawgiver as signs of denial, and committing them renders one a disbeliever regardless of inner state.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does committing an act like prostration to an idol make one a disbeliever?
A) Because it always indicates inner disbelief.
B) Because the Lawgiver has designated it as a sign of denial.
C) Because the person doubts his faith.
D) Because it is a major sin.

Key Takeaway
Outward acts designated by the Lawgiver as signs of denial are sufficient to render the doer a disbeliever, regardless of inner state.

?
Have you ever committed an act that you knew was a clear sign of disbelief according to the Qur’an or Sunnah, yet excused yourself because you 'felt' iman in your heart? How does this lesson hold you accountable for that act, and what concrete step will you take today to repent and never return to it?
?
When you observe Muslims committing major sins like shirk or mockery of religion, do you immediately assume they still have faith because they say they believe? How does this lesson challenge you to take seriously the outward signs that the Lawgiver has set as boundaries between iman and kufr?
?

Conditions for Acceptance of Good Deeds
Explanation of conditions for acceptance of good deeds and forgiveness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni condition for the acceptance of good deeds and forgiveness of sins, and how it differs from the Murji'a unconditional view.

Core Idea
Good deeds are accepted only with conditions and freedom from invalidators—not automatically.

This passage from Manah al-Rawdh al-Azhar explains the Sunni position on the acceptance of good deeds and forgiveness of sins. The author contrasts it with the Murji'a view, which claims unconditional acceptance. Instead, the Sunni view holds that deeds are accepted only if they meet specific conditions and are not invalidated before death. This lesson unpacks those conditions and the evidence from Quran and Hadith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author's direct rejection of the Murji'a position at the start.
2
Identify the conditions for acceptance
Look for the specific conditions mentioned: performing a good deed with all its conditions, free from both apparent defects and inner invalidating meanings (like disbelief, self-admiration, showing off), and not invalidating it before death.
The passage emphasizes that deeds are not automatically accepted.
3
Understand the refutation of an incorrect commentator
The author criticizes a commentator who included bad morals and other sins as invalidators, stating this is based on Mu'tazila principles, not Sunni.
This highlights the importance of staying within Sunni methodology.
4
Examine the interpretation of the envy hadith
The author explains that the hadith about envy consuming good deeds is interpreted as envy leading to sins that offset good deeds, not direct nullification.
This maintains the principle that good deeds are not invalidated by mere inner states without actions.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes by affirming that God does not waste the reward of believers and accepts their deeds by His grace, citing Quranic verses.
This gives hope while maintaining the need for conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا نقول: إن حسناتنا مقبولة وسيئاتنا مغفورة كقول المرجئة، ولكن نقول: من عمل حسنة بشرائطها خالية عن العيوب المفسدة والمعاني المبطلة ولم يبطلها حتى خرج من الدنيا فإن الله تعالى لا يضيعها بل يقبلها منه ويثيبه عليها، ولا نقول: إن حسناتنا مقبولة أي مبرورة وسيئاتنا مغفورة أي البتة كقول المرجئة بالهمزة والياء، ولكن نقول أي بل نعتقد المسألة مبينة مفصلة كما أوضحه بقوله: من عمل حسنة بشرائطها أي بجميع شرائطها كما في نسخة، أي واقعة بجميع مصححاتها في الابتداء خالية عن العيوب المفسدة أي الظاهرية والمعاني المبطلة أي الباطنية في الانتهاء كالكفر والعجب والرياء لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَنْ يَكْفُرْ بِالْإِيمَانِ فَقَدْ حَبِطَ عَمَلُهُ ﴾ [المائدة:ه]، وقوله تعالى: يَايُّهَا الَّذِينَ مَنُوا لا تُبْطِلُوا صَدَقَتِكُمْ بِالمَن وَالأذى كَالَّذِي يُنْفِقُ مَاله ي ناء الناس الآية [البقرة : ٢٦٤]. وأما قول الشارح وكالأخلاق السيئة وغيرها من المعصية فغير جار على مذهب أهل السنة والجماعة، بل مبني على قواعد المعتزلة، ثم ما ورد من نحو قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: الحسد يأكل الحسنات كما تأكل النار الحطب. فمؤوّل بأن الحسد غالبا يحمل الحاسد على ارتكاب سيئات بالنسبة إلى المحسود ، فيُعطى له من حسنات يعملها الحاسد في اليوم الموعود ولم يبطلها تأكيد لما قبلها وتأييد لتعلق ما بعدها حتى خرج من الدنيا وفيه إيماء إلى أنه مادام فيها فهو في خطر من إبطال الطاعة وإفسادها فإن الله تعالى لا يضيعها بتخفيف الياء وتشديدها، وذلك لقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يُضِيعُ أَجْرَ الْمُحْسِنِينَ ﴾ [التوبة:١٢٠]، وفي آية أخرى: وَأَنَّ اللهَ لَا يُضِيعُ أَجْرَ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ ﴾ [آل عمران: ۱۷۱] ، بل يقبلها منه أي بفضله وكرمه ويثيبه عليها أي بمقتضى وعده وحكمه.
We do not say: Our good deeds are accepted and our evil deeds are forgiven, like the saying of the Murji'a; rather we say: Whoever does a good deed with its conditions, free from corrupting defects and invalidating meanings, and does not invalidate it until he leaves the world, then Allah the Exalted does not waste it, rather He accepts it from him and rewards him for it. And we do not say: Our good deeds are accepted, i.e., blessed, and our evil deeds are forgiven, i.e., absolutely, like the saying of the Murji'a (with hamza and ya). Rather we say: we believe the matter is explained in detail, as he clarified by his saying: 'Whoever does a good deed with its conditions' i.e., with all its conditions as in one copy, i.e., occurring with all its rectifiers at the beginning, free from corrupting defects i.e., apparent ones, and invalidating meanings i.e., hidden ones at the end, such as disbelief, self-admiration, and showing off; due to His statement, the Exalted: {And whoever disbelieves in faith, his deed has become worthless} [al-Ma'ida 5:5], and His statement, the Exalted: {O you who have believed, do not invalidate your charity with reminders and injury, like the one who spends his wealth to be seen by people} [al-Baqara 2:264]. As for the statement of the commentator: 'and like bad morals and other sins' it does not run on the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a, but rather is based on the principles of the Mu'tazila. Then what has been reported like his saying, peace be upon him: 'Envy consumes good deeds as fire consumes wood' is interpreted that envy often drives the envier to commit evils against the envied, so he is given some of the good deeds the envier does on the Promised Day. And 'he did not invalidate it' is an emphasis for what preceded and a support for the attachment of what follows until he leaves the world, and in it is a hint that as long as he is in it he is in danger of invalidating obedience and corrupting it. For Allah the Exalted does not waste it (with tashdid and takhfif of the ya), and that is due to His statement: {Indeed, Allah does not waste the reward of the doers of good} [al-Tawba 9:120], and in another verse: {And that Allah does not waste the reward of the believers} [Al 'Imran 3:171]; rather He accepts it from him, i.e., by His bounty and generosity, and rewards him for it, i.e., according to His promise and decree.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the Sunni position regarding the acceptance of good deeds and forgiveness of sins, contrasting it with the Murji'a view. It begins by rejecting the unconditional claim of the Murji'a that all good deeds are accepted and all sins are forgiven. Instead, the author states a conditional position: a good deed is accepted only if performed with all its conditions, free from both outward corrupting defects and inward invalidating meanings (like disbelief, self-admiration, showing off), and if the person does not invalidate it before death. The passage then criticizes a commentator who included bad morals and other sins as invalidators, saying this is based on Mu'tazila principles, not Sunni. It also interprets the hadith about envy consuming good deeds: envy leads to sins that may offset good deeds, but it does not directly nullify them. Finally, it emphasizes that God does not waste the reward of believers and accepts their deeds by His grace.

Refutation of the Murji'ah on Acceptance of Deeds
Refutation of Murji'ah and clarification of conditions for deed acceptance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct Ahl al-Sunnah position on the acceptance of good deeds, the conditions required, and how it differs from the Murji'ah and other deviant views.

Core Idea
Good deeds are accepted only if performed with all conditions and free from nullifiers until death.

The commentator begins by distancing Ahl al-Sunnah from the Murji'ah, who claim all good deeds are accepted without conditions. Instead, he lays out a precise doctrine: acceptance depends on fulfilling conditions, avoiding corrupting defects and nullifying meanings, and not invalidating the deed before death. This passage establishes the balanced Sunni position between despair and presumption.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two positions
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the commentator contrasts the Murji'ah statement with the Ahl al-Sunnah statement. The Murji'ah say all good deeds are accepted and sins forgiven; Ahl al-Sunnah says acceptance is conditional.
The Murji'ah are mentioned with hamza and ya.
2
List the conditions and nullifiers
From the passage, extract the conditions (shurut) and the nullifying factors (uyub mufsida, ma'ani mubtila). Look for the specific examples like kufr, 'ujb, riya'.
The passage also refutes including bad morals as nullifiers.
3
Understand the refutation and the hadith explanation
The commentator criticizes another scholar for adding bad morals as nullifiers, calling it a Mu'tazili view. Then he explains the hadith about envy: envy leads to sins, not direct nullification. Finally, note the emphasis that deeds are not wasted if one dies without nullifying them.
This clarifies the danger of invalidating deeds during life.

And we do not say: that our good deeds are accepted and our sins are forgiven like the saying of the Murji'ah, but we say: Whoever does a good deed with its conditions, free from corrupting defects and nullifying meanings, and does not nullify it until he leaves the world, then Allah the Exalted does not waste it, but rather accepts it from him and rewards him for it. And we do not say: that our good deeds are accepted, i.e., blessed, and our sins are forgiven, i.e., absolutely, like the saying of the Murji'ah (with hamza and ya), but we say, i.e., rather we believe the matter is explained in detail as he clarified with his saying: 'Whoever does a good deed with its conditions' i.e., with all its conditions as in one copy, i.e., occurring with all its correctives at the beginning, free from corrupting defects, i.e., apparent, and nullifying meanings, i.e., inward at the end, like disbelief, arrogance, and showing off, due to His saying the Exalted: 'And whoever disbelieves in faith, his work has indeed failed' [5:5], and His saying the Exalted: 'O you who have believed, do not invalidate your charities with reproach and injury, like one who spends his wealth to be seen by people' [2:264]. As for the commentator's saying: 'and like bad morals and other acts of disobedience,' it is not in accordance with the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunnah wal-Jama'ah, but rather is based on the principles of the Mu'tazila. Then what has been reported like his saying, peace be upon him: 'Envy consumes good deeds just as fire consumes wood' is interpreted as meaning that envy often leads the envier to commit sins against the envied, so he is given from the good deeds that the envier performs on the promised day. 'And does not nullify it' is an emphasis on what came before and support for the connection of what comes after 'until he leaves the world,' and in it there is an indication that as long as he is in it, he is in danger of nullifying and corrupting obedience. Then Allah the Exalted does not waste it, with lightening the ya or doubling it, and that is due to His saying the Exalted: 'Indeed, Allah does not waste the reward of the doers of good' [9:120], and in another verse: 'And that Allah does not waste the reward of the believers' [3:171], but rather accepts it from him, i.e., by His bounty and generosity, and rewards him for it, i.e., according to His promise and judgment.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation and clarification. It begins by defining the correct Ahl al-Sunnah position against the Murji'ah, who would say all good deeds are accepted and sins forgiven unconditionally. The commentator insists that acceptance of good deeds is conditional: they must be performed with all their conditions (shurut), free from external corrupting defects (al-uyub al-mufsida) and internal nullifying aspects (al-ma'ani al-mubtila) such as disbelief, arrogance, and showing off (riya'). The proof texts are Quranic verses warning that disbelief nullifies deeds and that showing off invalidates charity. The commentator then refutes a misinterpretation by 'the commentator' (likely a previous commentator) who included bad morals and minor sins as nullifiers, stating that this contradicts Ahl al-Sunnah and aligns with Mu'tazilism. The hadith about envy is explained away: envy leads the envier to commit sins, and the reward is transferred to the envied on the Day of Judgment, not that envy directly nullifies deeds. The passage stresses that as long as the believer is alive, there is danger of nullification, but if he dies without invalidating the deed, Allah does not waste it and rewards out of His bounty.

Faith and Disbelief
beginner
5 min
Riya' Nullifies the Reward of Deeds
Commentator explains riya' nullifies reward and incurs sin with proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that riya' (showing off) and sum'ah (seeking reputation) nullify the reward of an act and incur sin, based on Quran and hadith.

Core Idea
Showing off destroys the reward and brings sin instead.

This lesson examines the spiritual danger of riya' and sum'ah in acts of worship. The commentator explains that even a tiny amount of insincerity can ruin the entire deed, turning potential reward into sin. We will explore the textual evidence and the conditions under which nullification occurs.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the key terms riya', sum'ah, ikhlas. Follow the argument from definition to proof.
Pay attention to the Quranic verse and hadith cited.
2
Understand the core rule
The passage states that if riya' occurs in a deed at its beginning or during it before completion, it nullifies the reward entirely and instead records sin.
This applies whether riya' is the sole motive or mixed with sincere intention.
3
Grasp the proof texts
The Quranic verse (18:110) forbids any shirk, clear or hidden. The hadiths reinforce that Allah does not accept deeds with even an atom's weight of riya'.
The second hadith is a direct warning against mixing intentions.
4
Address a common misunderstanding
Some think only deliberate initial riya' matters, but the commentary clarifies that riya' inserted during the act also nullifies reward.
5
Reflect and apply
Consider how this teaching impacts your own worship. Ask yourself: what is my intention? How can I purify it?
Sincerity (ikhlas) is the foundation of accepted deeds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرياء إذا وقع في عمل من الأعمال فإنه يبطل أجره . والرياء وفي معناه السمعة، وقد توسع في إطلاق أحدهما وإرادة كل منهما لمآل أمرهما إلى عدم الإخلاص، حيث المرائي يظهر العمل ليراه الناس ويستحسنوه في مقام الإيناس والمسمع يفعل الفعل ليسمعه الخلق وليس في غرضه رضى الحق، إذا وقع في عمل من الأعمال أي في ابتدائه أو أثنائه قبل الإكمال فإنه يبطل أجره أي أجر ذلك العمل، بل يثبت وزره حيث ظلم نفسه بوضع الشيء في غير موضعه، قال الله تعالى: ﴿فَمَنْ كَانَ يَرْجُوا لِقَاءَ رَبِّهِ فَلْيَعْمَلْ عَمَلاً صَالِحاً وَلَا يُشْرِكْ بِعِبَادَةِ رَبِّهِ أَحَداً﴾ [الكهف: ١١٠]، أي لا شركا جليا ولا خفيا، وفيه إيماء إلى أنه إذا قصد الرياء والسمعة وقصد الطاعة والعبادة جميعا يوصف بالشركة مطلقا لغلبة أحدهما على الآخر، أو التسوية بينهما، فإنه يبطل أجره ويثبت وزره لعموم حديث: ((من كان أشرك أحداً في عمل عمله الله فليطلب ثوابه مما سواه، فإن الله أغنى الشركاء عن الشرك)). وكذا حديث: ((لا يقبل الله عملا فيه مقدار ذرّة من الرياء)).
And showing off (riya') – if it occurs in a deed among deeds, then it nullifies its reward. And showing off, and in its meaning is seeking reputation (sum'ah), and they have expanded in the usage of each and intending each, because their ultimate end is lack of sincerity, where the one who shows off displays the action for people to see and approve in the context of familiarity, and the one seeking reputation does the action for people to hear, and not in his aim is the pleasure of the Truth. If it occurs in a deed among deeds, i.e., at its beginning or during it before completion, then it nullifies its reward, i.e., the reward of that action; rather, it establishes its sin, as he has wronged himself by placing a thing in other than its place. Allah the Exalted said: 'So whoever hopes for the meeting with his Lord, let him do righteous work and not associate in the worship of his Lord anyone' [18:110], i.e., no clear shirk nor hidden. And in it is an indication that if he intends showing off and reputation and intends obedience and worship together, he is described as having shirk absolutely due to the dominance of one over the other, or equality between them; then it nullifies his reward and establishes his sin, due to the general meaning of the hadith: 'Whoever associates someone in an action that Allah did, let him seek reward from other than Him, for Allah is the most self-sufficient of partners from shirk.' And likewise the hadith: 'Allah does not accept an action in which there is the weight of an atom of showing off.'
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal explanation of the effect of riya' (showing off) on acts of worship. It begins by stating that riya' in any deed nullifies its reward. The commentator then clarifies that 'sum'ah' (seeking reputation through hearing) is similar, and both lack sincerity (ikhlas). The point is that the actor is not seeking Allah's pleasure but people's approval. The nullification applies whether the riya' occurs at the beginning or during the act before completion. Not only is the reward lost, but the person incurs sin (wizr) for misplacing the act. The proof text is the Quranic verse commanding righteous work without any shirk, whether clear or hidden. The passage further explains that if the person intends both riya' and worship together, it is considered shirk (associating partners) because of the dominance of one intention or equality. Two supporting hadiths are cited: one where the one who associates partners is told to seek reward from that partner, and another saying Allah does not accept deeds with even a tiny amount of riya'. Thus, the lesson is that riya' completely spoils the deed and brings sin, not reward.

Key Terms
رياء
سمعة
إخلاص
إبطال الأجر
وزر
شرك خفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Riya' only nullifies reward if it is the sole intention from the start of the deed.
Correction: The passage clarifies that riya' nullifies reward even if it occurs during the deed before completion, and even if mixed with sincere intention, due to the dominance of one over the other or equality.

Checkpoint
When does riya' nullify the reward of a deed according to the passage?
A) Only if it occurs at the beginning of the deed.
B) Only if it occurs after the deed is completed.
C) If it occurs at the beginning or during the deed before completion.
D) Only if it is the sole intention without any worship.

Key Takeaway
Riya' and sum'ah nullify the reward of any righteous deed and bring sin, even if mixed with sincerity.

?
The lesson teaches that even a mix of sincerity and showing off makes the deed unacceptable. Are you willing to examine your deepest motives, even the ones you hide from yourself, and repent from any hidden shirk?
