Faith and Disbelief
intermediate
5 min
Faith Does Not Increase or Decrease: Abu Hanifa's Proof
Abu Hanifa's logical and scriptural proof that faith's essence is unchanging.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hanifa's logical and scriptural argument for why faith in its essence is unchanging, and why this distinction matters for the status of sinful believers.

Core Idea
Faith cannot increase because increase requires decrease in disbelief, but one cannot be both.

Abu Hanifa's argument from al-Wasiyya is a foundational text in Sunni theology. It tackles the question of whether faith can change, a key issue against those who said faith increases and decreases. The argument uses logical consistency and Quranic proof to show that the reality of faith is fixed. In this lesson, we will read his words and unpack the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Start with the cautionary quote
Read Imam Muhammad's statement about comparing one's faith to Jibril's. Understand why he disliked that expression.
This sets the stage: faith is equal in essence but not in degree or manifestation.
2
Examine the logical proof
Read Abu Hanifa's core argument: increase in faith requires decrease in disbelief, and vice versa. Since one cannot be both a believer and disbeliever simultaneously, faith cannot genuinely change.
Focus on the conditional 'لا يتصور زيادة الإيمان إلا بنقصان الكفر'.
3
Look at the Quranic evidence
Notice the two verses Abu Hanifa cites to affirm that believers are truly believers without doubt, and disbelievers are truly disbelievers.
The verses remove ambiguity about the status of believers.
4
Apply to the case of sinners
Read the final sentence about sinners of the Muslim community. Understand that they remain true believers, not disbelievers.
This refutes Khawarij and Mu'tazila who said major sins remove faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن هنا قال الإمام محمد رحمه الله على ما ذكره في "الخلاصة" عنه: «أكره أن يقول إيماني كإيمان جبرائيل عليه السلام ولكن يقول آمنت بما آمن به جبرائیل علیه السلام»، انتهى. وذلك أن إيمانه كإيمان جبرائيل عليه السلام من جميع الوجوه، وليس الأمر كذلك لما أن الأول يوهم هو الفرق البين بينهما هنالك. قال الإمام الأعظم رحمه الله في كتابه "الوصية": ثم الإيمان لا يزيد ولا ينقص ()، لأنه لا ينقص)، يتصور زيادة الإيمان إلا بنقصان الكفر ، ولا يتصوّر نقصان الإيمان إلا بزيادة الكفر، فكيف يجوز أن يكون الشخص الواحد في حالة واحدة مؤمنا وكافرا، والمؤمن مؤمن حقا والكافر كافر حقا، وليس في إيمان المؤمن شك، كما أنه ليس في كفر الكافر شك، لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَ أُولَئِكَ هُمُ الْمُؤْمِنُونَ حَقَّا [الأنفال : ٤]، أي في موضع، وأُولَبِكَ هُمُ الْكَفِرُونَ حَقًّا [النساء:١٥١]، أي في محل آخر. والعاصون من أمة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم كلهم مؤمنون حقا وليسوا بكافرين، أي حقا. انتهى.
And from here Imam Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said, as mentioned in al-Khulasa concerning him: 'I dislike that one should say: My faith is like the faith of Jibril (peace be upon him), but one should say: I believe in what Jibril (peace be upon him) believed.' End quote. That is because his faith is like the faith of Jibril (peace be upon him) in all respects, but it is not so, because the first expression suggests the clear difference between them there. The Great Imam (may Allah have mercy on him) said in his book al-Wasiyya: 'Then faith does not increase nor decrease ( ), because it does not decrease. Increase of faith can be conceived only by decrease of disbelief, and decrease of faith can be conceived only by increase of disbelief. So how can it be permissible that one single person in one state be a believer and a disbeliever? The believer is truly a believer and the disbeliever is truly a disbeliever. There is no doubt in the faith of the believer, just as there is no doubt in the disbelief of the disbeliever, due to His statement: “Those are the believers in truth” (al-Anfal:4) — in one place — and “Those are the disbelievers in truth” (al-Nisa:151) — in another place. And the sinners of the community of Muhammad (peace and blessings of Allah be upon him) are all truly believers and are not disbelievers — truly.' End quote.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage begins with a narration from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, a student of Abu Hanifa, who said in al-Khulasa: 'I dislike that one should say: My faith is like the faith of Jibril (peace be upon him), but one should say: I believe in what Jibril believed.' The reason given is that while in a sense the faith of any believer is like that of Jibril in terms of essence, the expression 'like the faith of Jibril' could wrongly imply equality in all respects, whereas there is a clear distinction between the two. The passage then presents the core argument from Abu Hanifa's work al-Wasiyya. Abu Hanifa states: 'Then faith does not increase nor decrease.' He provides a logical proof: an increase in faith can only be conceived if disbelief decreases, and a decrease in faith can only be conceived if disbelief increases. Since it is impossible for a single person to be both a believer and a disbeliever at the same time, faith cannot genuinely increase or decrease. Furthermore, he quotes Quranic verses: 'Those are the believers in truth' (al-Anfal:4) and 'Those are the disbelievers in truth' (al-Nisa:151), emphasizing that believers are truly believers without doubt, and disbelievers are truly disbelievers. Finally, he affirms that the sinners of the Muslim community are truly believers, not disbelievers, contrary to the Khawarij and Mu'tazila. This establishes that sin does not negate faith.

Key Terms
إيمان
كفر
زيادة
نقصان
حقا
عصاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Faith increases and decreases with actions or certainty, so its essence changes.
Correction: Abu Hanifa argues that increase in faith would require decrease in disbelief, which is impossible in one state. Therefore faith's essence is unchanging; what changes is its expressions or certainty, not the reality of iman.

Checkpoint
Why does Abu Hanifa argue that faith cannot increase or decrease?
A) Because increase would require decrease of disbelief, which is impossible in the same state.
B) Because faith is a simple concept without degrees.
C) Because the Quran explicitly says so.
D) Because the Prophet said so.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa establishes that the essence of faith is fixed and cannot genuinely increase or decrease, because any change would require a contradictory state of simultaneous belief and disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your faith cannot genuinely increase or decrease, what does that mean for how you approach your sins? Do you still feel the weight of your disobedience as a believer, or do you take comfort in the fact that your faith remains intact despite your shortcomings? How does this teaching change the way you view your own moments of weakness?
?
Abu Hanifa argues that it is impossible for a person to be both a believer and a disbeliever in one state. Yet, how often do you act as if you are in a middle ground—acknowledging Allah but living as if He is not watching? When you sin, do you treat it as a lapse in belief or as a failure of action? How does this lesson challenge you to align your outward actions with your inner faith?
?
The proof rests on the logical impossibility of combining iman and kufr. But what about nifaq (hypocrisy)? Are you ever guilty of performing acts of faith while harboring doubts or insincerity? How can you use this lesson to purify your intentions and ensure that your faith is not mixed with hypocrisy?

Faith and Disbelief

Interpreting Increase of Faith and the Sunni Position on Sinners
Explains Quranic and hadith evidence for faith's increase as certainty or actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position that sin does not negate faith and how verses and hadith about increase of faith are interpreted without implying change in faith's essence.

Core Idea
Disobedience does not destroy faith; scriptural 'increase' refers to certainty, actions, or objects of faith.

Abu Hanifa's statement that disobedience does not contradict faith is a foundational Sunni doctrine. However, Quranic verses and hadith that speak of faith increasing and decreasing might seem to challenge this. This lesson unpacks how the commentator reconciles these texts, showing that the increase refers to certainty, good deeds, or the number of beliefs, not the essence of faith itself.

Guided Path
1
Understand the doctrinal claim
The passage opens by stating that disobedience does not negate faith, which is the Sunni position against Khawarij and Mu'tazila. Note that this applies to the rank of perfection, not that sin is absent.
This sets up the need to interpret scriptural evidence.
2
Examine the Quranic interpretation
Read the explanation of verse 8:2 of the Quran. The commentator gives two possible meanings: increase in certainty (yaqin) or increase due to more revelation.
Both interpretations keep faith's essence unchanged.
3
Analyze the hadith explanation
The hadith says faith increases and decreases. The commentator explains this as referring to the consequences of good and bad deeds on entry to Paradise or Hell, not a change in faith itself.
The believer ultimately enters Paradise by faith despite initial punishment.
4
Grasp Abu Hanifa's resolution
Finally, note Abu Hanifa's point that faith increased as new obligations were revealed. The early believers believed in general, then each specific command, so faith increased in terms of objects of belief.
This shows increase is quantitative, not qualitative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأشار الإمام الأعظم رحمه الله بهذا الكلام إلى أن العصيان لا ينافي الإيمان، كما هو مذهب أهل السنة والجماعة، خلافا للخوارج والمعتزلة، فإنهما عندهم لا يجتمعان، ونحن نحمل هذا الحال على مقام الكمال، فإن نفي المعصية بالكلية من المؤمن كالمحال، وأما نحو قوله تعالى: وَإِذَا تُلِيَتْ عَلَيْهِمْ أَيْتُهُ زَادَتْهُمْ إِيمَانًا ﴾ [الأنفال: ٢]، فمعناه إيقانا، أو مؤوّل بأن المراد زيادة الإيمان بزيادة نزول المؤمن به أي .القرآن وأما قوله صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم لما سئل: إن الإيمان يزيد وينقص؟ : ((نعم، يزيد حتى يُدخل صاحبه الجنة، وينقص حتى يدخل صاحبه النار)). فمعناه أنه يزيد باعتبار أعماله الحسنة حتى يدخل صاحبه الجنة دخولاً أوليا، وينقص بارتكاب أعماله السيئة حتى يدخل صاحبه النار أولاً ، ثم يدخل الجنة بإيمانه آخرا، كما هو مقتضى مذهب أهل السنة والجماعة على أن التصديق من الكيفيات النفسية للإنسان، وهي على زيادة الإيمان محمولة على ما ذكره أبو حنيفة رحمه الله أنهم كانوا آمنوا في الجملة ثم يأتي فرض بعد فرض وكانوا يؤمنون بكل فرض خاص، وحاصله أنه كان يزيد بزيادة ما يجب به الإيمان... إلخ.
So the Great Imam (may Allah have mercy on him) indicated by this statement that disobedience does not contradict faith, as is the school of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a, contrary to the Khawarij and Mu'tazila, for according to them the two do not coexist. And we carry this situation to the rank of perfection, for the complete negation of disobedience from the believer is almost impossible. As for the saying of the Exalted: 'And when His verses are recited to them, they increase them in faith' (al-Anfal:2), its meaning is certainty (yaqin), or it is interpreted that what is meant is increase in faith by increase in the revelation of the object of faith, i.e., the Quran. And as for his statement (peace and blessings of Allah be upon him) when asked: 'Does faith increase and decrease?' He said: 'Yes, it increases until it admits its possessor into Paradise, and decreases until it admits its possessor into Hell.' Its meaning is that it increases by virtue of his good deeds until its possessor enters Paradise first, and decreases by committing his evil deeds until its possessor enters Hell first, then he enters Paradise by his faith later, as is required by the school of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a. And that assent (tasdiq) is among the psychological qualities of man, and they are carried upon the increase of faith according to what Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned: that they believed in general, then one obligation after another came and they believed in each specific obligation. The gist of it is that it increased by increase in what must be believed in... etc.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage serves as the commentator's explanation of Abu Hanifa's earlier statement. It opens by affirming that the Great Imam (Abu Hanifa) indicated by his words that disobedience (isyan) does not contradict faith – this is the position of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a, in opposition to the Khawarij and Mu'tazila, who hold that sin and faith cannot coexist. The author notes that we understand this situation as relating to the rank of perfection (kamal), because the complete absence of sin in a believer is nearly impossible. Then the passage addresses scriptural evidence that seems to suggest increase in faith. Regarding the Quranic verse 'And when His verses are recited to them, they increase them in faith' (al-Anfal:2), he gives two interpretations: either the increase refers to certainty (yaqin) becoming stronger, or the meaning is that faith increases because the object of faith (i.e., Quranic revelations) increases – the more revelation comes down, the more items to believe in. As for the hadith where the Prophet was asked whether faith increases and decreases, and he replied, 'Yes, it increases until it admits its possessor into Paradise, and decreases until it admits its possessor into Hell,' the commentator explains that this refers to the effects of good and bad deeds: good deeds cause an initial entry into Paradise, while bad deeds cause initial entry into Hell, but ultimately the believer enters Paradise due to his faith. This interpretation aligns with the Sunni position. Finally, the commentator notes that assent (tasdiq) is among the psychological qualities of a person, and the increase of faith is understood in light of Abu Hanifa's explanation: the early believers accepted faith in general terms, then as each new obligation was revealed, they believed in it specifically, thus faith increased in terms of the number of things to believe, not in its essential reality.

Key Terms
عصيان
إيمان
كفر
إيقان
تصديق
أعمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the hadith about faith increasing and decreasing means the essence of faith itself changes.
Correction: The commentator explains that the hadith refers to the consequences of good and bad deeds on entry into Paradise or Hell, not a change in the reality of faith.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, how does the Quranic verse (al-Anfal:2) 'increase them in faith' get interpreted?
A) Faith increases in its essence.
B) Increase refers to certainty (yaqin) or to more revelation.
C) It is abrogated.
D) It applies only to prophets.

Key Takeaway
Faith's essence is immutable; scriptural references to increase or decrease pertain to certainty, deeds, or the number of beliefs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever feel that your iman has completely left you, or that you are no longer a true believer? This lesson teaches that disobedience does not negate faith. How does that truth change the way you view your own sins and your relationship with Allah? What will you do today to remind yourself that your faith remains even when you fall short?
?
The hadith says faith can decrease until it admits its possessor into Hell first. Have you ever felt your faith weakening? What concrete steps can you take this week to strengthen your iman through good deeds, knowing that they increase faith's consequences?
?
The Quran says the believers' faith increases when they hear verses. When was the last time reading or hearing the Quran increased your certainty or moved you to action? If your response is 'not recently,' what is blocking that increase? Commit to a specific verse or surah to ponder today to seek that increase.

The Sinful Believer is a True Believer
Establishes that a sinful Muslim remains a true believer, refuting doubt in faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to Sunni Islam, a person who commits sins remains a true believer and is not a disbeliever.

Core Idea
Sinners from the Muslim community are true believers, not disbelievers.

This lesson introduces a foundational Sunni doctrine: that a Muslim who commits major sins does not lose faith. The source passage, from a classical creedal text, states this clearly and provides Quranic evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the assertive tone and the use of 'truly' (haqqan).
2
Identify the main claim
The passage begins by stating that a person cannot be both a believer and a disbeliever in one state. Find this statement and understand its implication.
This directly opposes any view that mixes faith and disbelief.
3
Examine the Quranic proof
Two verses are cited: Quran 8:4 and 4:151. Notice how each is used to prove that believers and disbelievers are each truly what they are.
The verses are presented as clear evidence, leaving no room for doubt.
4
Apply to sinful Muslims
The passage concludes that sinners from the Muslim community are all true believers. Reflect on how this applies to your own understanding of fellow Muslims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مذهب أن يكون الشخص الواحد في حالة واحدة مؤمنا وكافرا، والمؤمن مؤمن حقا والكافر كافر حقا، وليس في إيمان المؤمن شك، كما أنه ليس في كفر الكافر شك، لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَ أُولَئِكَ هُمُ الْمُؤْمِنُونَ حَقًّا﴾ [الأنفال : ٤]، أي في موضع، ﴿وَ أُولَئِكَ هُمُ الْكَافِرُونَ حَقًّا﴾ [النساء:١٥١]، أي في محل آخر. والعاصون من أمة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم كلهم مؤمنون حقا وليسوا بكافرين، أي حقا. انتهى. فأشار الإمام الأعظم رحمه الله بهذا الكلام إلى أن العصيان لا ينافي الإيمان، كما هو أهل السنة والجماعة، خلافا للخوارج والمعتزلة، فإنهما عندهم لا يجتمعان، ونحن نحمل هذا الحال على مقام الكمال، فإن نفي المعصية بالكلية من المؤمن كالمحال، وأما نحو قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِذَا تُلِيَتْ عَلَيْهِمْ آيَاتُهُ زَادَتْهُمْ إِيمَانًا﴾ [الأنفال: ٢]، فمعناه إيقانا، أو مؤوّل بأن المراد زيادة الإيمان بزيادة نزول المؤمن به أي القرآن. وأما قوله صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم لما سئل: إن الإيمان يزيد وينقص؟ : ((نعم، يزيد حتى يُدخل صاحبه الجنة، وينقص حتى يدخل صاحبه النار)). فمعناه أنه يزيد باعتبار أعماله الحسنة حتى يدخل صاحبه الجنة دخولاً أوليا، وينقص بارتكاب أعماله السيئة حتى يدخل صاحبه النار أولاً، ثم يدخل الجنة بإيمانه آخرا، كما هو مقتضى مذهب أهل السنة والجماعة على أن التصديق من الكيفيات النفسية للإنسان، وهي على زيادة الإيمان محمولة على ما ذكره أبو حنيفة رحمه الله أنهم كانوا آمنوا في الجملة ثم يأتي فرض بعد فرض وكانوا يؤمنون بكل فرض خاص، وحاصله أنه كان يزيد بزيادة ما يجب به الإيمان... إلخ.
The doctrine is that one person cannot be a believer and a disbeliever in one state. The believer is truly a believer and the disbeliever is truly a disbeliever. There is no doubt in the faith of the believer, just as there is no doubt in the disbelief of the disbeliever, due to His statement, the Exalted: 'And those are the believers in truth' (al-Anfal 8:4), i.e., in one place, and 'And those are the disbelievers in truth' (al-Nisa 4:151), i.e., in another place. And the sinners from the nation of Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace, are all truly believers and are not disbelievers, i.e., truly. End. So the Greatest Imam, may God have mercy on him, indicated by this speech that disobedience does not contradict faith, as is the way of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a, contrary to the Khawarij and the Mu'tazila, for with them the two do not combine. And we interpret this situation as [referring to] the station of perfection, for the absolute negation of disobedience from the believer is impossible. As for the like of His statement, the Exalted: 'And when His verses are recited to them, they increase them in faith' (al-Anfal 8:2), its meaning is certainty, or it is interpreted that what is meant is the increase of faith by the increase of revelation of that which one believes in, i.e., the Qur'an. And as for his statement, may God bless him and grant him peace, when he was asked: 'Does faith increase and decrease?' He said: 'Yes, it increases until it enters its companion into Paradise, and it decreases until it enters its companion into the Fire.' So its meaning is that it increases by virtue of his good deeds until it enters its companion into Paradise as a first entry, and it decreases by his commission of evil deeds until it enters its companion into the Fire first, then he enters Paradise by his faith after that, as is required by the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a, that affirmation (al-tasdiq) is among the psychic qualities of man, and it is carried upon the increase of faith as mentioned by Abu Hanifa, may God have mercy on him: that they believed in general, then one duty after another came, and they would believe in every specific duty, and its gist is that it would increase with the increase of what one must believe in, etc.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage defends the Sunni position that a Muslim who commits major sins remains a true believer, contrary to the Khawarij (who declare such a person a disbeliever) and the Mu'tazila (who place him in an intermediate state). The author begins by quoting a statement (likely from Imam Abu Hanifa) that a person cannot be both a believer and a disbeliever in one state; believers and disbelievers are each truly what they are, without doubt. The evidence is two Quranic verses: one calls the believers 'true believers' and another calls the disbelievers 'true disbelievers', each in different contexts. The point is that sinners from the Muslim community are still true believers, not disbelievers. The author then explains that the Great Imam (Abu Hanifa) indicated by this that disobedience does not contradict faith—this is the position of Ahl al-Sunna, against the Khawarij and Mu'tazila. He clarifies that the statement about being 'truly' refers to the station of perfection, because it is impossible to completely negate sin from a believer. Next, he addresses two scriptural proofs that seem to imply faith increases and decreases. The verse 'when His verses are recited to them, they increase them in faith' is interpreted as meaning increase in certainty (yaqin), or as an increase in the objects of faith (as more Quran was revealed). The hadith in which the Prophet said faith increases and decreases is interpreted in the context of deeds: faith increases due to good deeds, leading to an initial entry into Paradise, and decreases due to evil deeds, leading to an initial entry into Hellfire, but ultimately the person enters Paradise due to his faith. This is consistent with the Sunni view that affirmation (tasdiq) is a psychic quality, and increase in faith is understood in the sense of increased obligations to believe in, as Abu Hanifa said that the early believers believed in general, and then as each new duty was revealed, they believed in it specifically. Thus the increase is in the objects of faith rather than the essence of faith itself.

Key Terms
إيمان
كفر
عصيان
حقا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that committing sins removes a person from faith entirely.
Correction: The passage clarifies that sinners remain believers in truth, though they may be punished.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of a Muslim who commits major sins?
A) He becomes a disbeliever.
B) He is in an intermediate state between faith and disbelief.
C) He remains a true believer, though sinful.
D) His faith is completely nullified.

Key Takeaway
A Muslim who sins is still a true believer, as affirmed by Quranic verses and the Sunni creed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on your own sins: do you ever doubt your faith because of them? After reading this passage, how should that doubt be addressed? What specific action will you take this week to affirm your faith despite your shortcomings?
?
If you truly believe that sinners remain believers, how does that change the way you view fellow Muslims who struggle with major sins? Will you look at them with mercy or judgment? What will you do to practically support one such person this week?

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate
5 min
Refuting the Khawarij and Mu'tazila
Explains that disobedience does not contradict faith, contrary to Khawarij and Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sunni Islam rejects the Khawarij and Mu'tazila positions that sin negates faith.

Core Idea
Disobedience does not contradict faith; sin and faith can coexist.

Having established that sinners are believers, the text now directly addresses the opposing views of the Khawarij and Mu'tazila. It explains that the Great Imam (Abu Hanifa) indicated that disobedience does not negate faith, and that the statement about being 'truly' refers to the station of perfection, since complete sinlessness is impossible for believers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the reference to the Great Imam and the mention of two sects.
2
Identify the two opposing sects
The text names the Khawarij and the Mu'tazila. Recall their positions: Khawarij declare sinners disbelievers; Mu'tazila place them in an intermediate state.
These sects both deny that faith and sin can coexist.
3
Understand the Sunni response
The passage states that disobedience does not contradict faith, contrary to those sects. It also adds a nuance: the statement about being 'truly' refers to the station of perfection, because it is impossible for believers to be completely sinless.
This nuance prevents misunderstanding that believers are sinless.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this doctrine affects your view of fellow Muslims who commit sins. They remain brothers in faith.

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate
6 min
Increase and Decrease of Faith
Interprets verses and hadith about increase and decrease of faith as referring to certainty or objects of faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sunni scholars interpret verses and hadith about faith increasing and decreasing, showing they refer to certainty or objects of faith.

Core Idea
Faith increases in certainty or in objects of belief, not in its essence.

The source passage addresses two scriptural proofs that seem to imply faith fluctuates: a Quranic verse about increase in faith and a hadith about increase and decrease. The text provides two interpretations: that it means increase in certainty (yaqin), or increase in the objects of faith (new revelations). It also cites Abu Hanifa's view that faith increases with new obligations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two scriptural references: Quran 8:2 and the hadith about increase and decrease.
2
Understand the first interpretation of Quran 8:2
The verse is interpreted as meaning increase in certainty (yaqin), or as increase in the objects of faith (the Qur'an revealed progressively).
This shows that the increase is not in the essence of faith but in its strength or content.
3
Interpret the hadith on increase and decrease
The hadith is explained as referring to the effects of deeds: good deeds lead to an initial entry into Paradise, bad deeds to an initial entry into Hell, but ultimately faith saves. The increase/decrease is in the consequences, not in the faith itself.
4
Review Abu Hanifa's explanation
Abu Hanifa said that early believers believed in general, then as each new duty was revealed, they believed in it specifically. Thus faith increased by increase of obligations.
This reinforces that increase is in the objects of belief.

Correction: The passage clarifies that increase refers to certainty (yaqin) or to the objects of faith, not a change in the core affirmation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, one interpretation of 'increase in faith' in Quran 8:2 is:
A) Increase in the number of believers
B) Increase in certainty (yaqin)
C) Increase in wealth
D) Increase in lifespan

Key Takeaway
Scriptural references to increase and decrease in faith are understood by Sunni scholars as referring to certainty or to the objects of faith, not to a change in the essence of faith itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you feel your faith has diminished or that you have lost it? How does this lesson's interpretation of the hadith challenge that feeling, and what must you do to hold fast to the core affirmation even when you fall short?
?
The early believers increased their faith by believing in each new revelation. Are you actively seeking to increase your faith by learning new aspects of your religion, or have you become stagnant? What specific step will you take this week to learn something new about what you must believe?
?
If your good deeds affect your initial entry but your final salvation is secured by your faith, how does that change the way you view your daily actions? Are you relying on your deeds alone, or do you truly trust in the core belief that saves? What one habit can you change to rely more on faith than on your own performance?

Equality in the Foundation of Faith
Explains equality in faith and tawhid and the analogy of sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that all believers share the same essence of faith and tawhid, but faith can increase and decrease in strength, and how obedience and disobedience affect the heart.

Core Idea
All believers are equal in the root of faith; the difference lies in its strength and light.

This lesson begins with a foundational principle: every believer is equal in the essence of faith and tawhid. Yet faith is not static—it waxes and wanes with deeds. The author uses the analogy of sight to illustrate how the same reality can vary vastly in degrees.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the key terms and the flow from statement to analogy.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two main statements
First, the author says believers are equal in faith and tawhid. Then he says faith accepts increase and decrease in strength. Mark these two points.
These are not contradictory; they describe different aspects.
3
Reflect on the analogy of sight
The author compares faith to sight: all who see share the essence of sight, but they differ in strength. Think about how this clarifies the nature of faith.
The analogy helps explain how equality in essence allows for individual differences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمؤمنون مستوون في الإيمان والتوحيد تقبل الزيادة والنقصان باعتبار القوة والضعف في مراتب الإيقان، ثم الطاعة والعبادة ثمرة الإيمان ونتيجة الإيقان وتنوّر القلب بنور العرفان بخلاف المعصية فإنها تسوّد القلب وتضعف محبة الربّ، وربما يجره مداومة العصيان إلى ظلمات الكفران فإن الصغيرة تجر إلى الكبيرة، والكبيرة إلى الكفر، فنسأل الله العافية وحسن الخاتمة. و(والمؤمنون مستوون) أي متساوون في (الإيمان أي في أصله (والتوحيد) أي في نفسه، وإنما قيدنا بهما؛ لأن الكفر مع الإيمان كالعمي مع البصر، ولا شك أن البصراء يختلفون في قوة البصر وضعفه، فمنهم الأخفش والأعشى، ومن يرى الخط الثخين دون الرقيق إلا بزجاجة ونحوها، ومن يرى عن قرب زائد على العادة وآخر بضده. ومن هنا قال محمد رحمه الله على ما تقدم أكره أن يقول إيماني كإيمان جبرائيل عليه السلام، بل يقول آمنت بما آمن به جبرائیل علیه السلام. وكذا لا يجوز أن يقول أحد: إيماني كإيمان الأنبياء عليهم السلام، بل ولا ينبغى أن يقول : إيماني كإيمان أبي بكر وعمر رضي الله عنهما وأمثالهما، فإنّ تفاوت نور كلمة التوحيد في قلوب أهلها لا يحصيه إلا الله سبحانه، فمن الناس من نورها في قلبه كالشمس ومنهم كالقمر، ومنهم كالكوكب الدري، ومنهم كالمشعل العظيم، وآخر كالسراج الضعيف لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام وذلك أضعف الإيمان. وقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: المؤمن القوي أحب إلى الله من المؤمن الضعيف. والقوة تشمل القوة الظاهرية العملية والقوة الباطنية العلمية، وهو على منوال هذه الأنوار
And the believers are equal in faith and tawhid; they accept increase and decrease according to strength and weakness in the ranks of certainty. Then obedience and worship are the fruit of faith and the result of certainty, and the heart is illuminated with the light of gnosis, unlike disobedience, for it blackens the heart and weakens love of the Lord, and perhaps persistence in disobedience drags one to the darknesses of disbelief, for a small sin drags to a large one, and a large one to disbelief. So we ask God for well-being and a good end. And 'the believers are equal' means they are equal in faith, i.e., in its origin, and in tawhid, i.e., in itself. We only restricted [it] to these two because disbelief with faith is like blindness with sight; there is no doubt that those who see differ in the strength and weakness of sight—among them are the weak-sighted and the night-blind, and those who see thick lines but not thin except with a lens and the like, and those who see from a distance unusually and another whose sight is opposite. Hence Muhammad, may God have mercy on him, said, according to what has preceded: I dislike that one should say my faith is like the faith of Gabriel, peace be upon him, but rather he should say: I believe in what Gabriel believed in. Likewise, it is not permissible for anyone to say: my faith is like the faith of the prophets, peace be upon them, and neither should one say: my faith is like the faith of Abu Bakr and Umar, may God be pleased with them, and their likes. For the variation of the light of the word of tawhid in the hearts of its people cannot be encompassed except by God, the Exalted. Among people, its light in his heart is like the sun, among them like the moon, among them like the brilliant planet, among them like the great torch, and another like the weak lamp, due to his statement, peace and blessings be upon him: 'That is the weakest of faith.' And his statement, peace and blessings be upon him: 'The strong believer is more beloved to God than the weak believer.' And strength includes apparent practical strength and inner knowledge-based strength, and it is patterned after these lights.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on the statement 'Believers are equal in faith and tawhid.' The author begins by affirming that believers are equal in the essence of faith (its origin) and in tawhid itself, yet these admit increase and decrease in terms of strength and weakness in the ranks of certainty (iqan). He then elaborates on the consequences: obedience and worship are the fruit of faith and certainty, illuminating the heart with the light of gnosis; disobedience, by contrast, blackens the heart and weakens love of God, and persistent sin can lead to disbelief itself, as small sins lead to major ones and major sins to disbelief. The author then unpacks the phrase 'the believers are equal' by explaining that they are equal in the foundation of faith and tawhid, not in their degrees. He uses an analogy: disbelief is like blindness, faith like sight. All who see share the reality of sight, but they differ in its strength and weakness—some have weak sight, some see only thick lines without glasses, some see very close or far. Similarly, believers share the essence of faith but differ in its strength. For this reason, Imam Muhammad (likely Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani) is reported to have disliked saying 'my faith is like the faith of Gabriel' and instead said 'I believe in what Gabriel believed in.' Likewise, one should not claim equality with the prophets or even the greatest Companions like Abu Bakr and Umar. The variation in the light of the word of tawhid (la ilaha illa Allah) in hearts is incalculable; the author gives a vivid simile: some hearts have light like the sun, some like the moon, some like a brilliant planet, some like a great torch, and some like a weak lamp. He supports this with two hadiths: one stating that certain actions are 'the weakest of faith,' and another that 'the strong believer is more beloved to God than the weak believer.' He concludes by noting that strength includes both outward, practical strength and inner, knowledge-based strength, and that this variation follows the pattern of these lights.

طاعة

عرفان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that all believers have exactly the same degree of faith, or that faith is completely uniform.
Correction: The passage teaches that believers are equal in the essence of faith and tawhid, but faith varies in strength and weakness. The sight analogy shows that a shared essence does not mean identical quality.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what analogy is used to explain that believers are equal in the origin of faith but differ in its strength?
A) Light and darkness
B) Sight and blindness
C) Water and thirst
D) Fire and heat

Key Takeaway
Faith is one in its essence but varies in strength, just as sight is one in reality but differs in sharpness among people.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your daily life, where are you actively seeking to increase the light of faith through obedience, and where are you neglecting it, allowing small compromises to darken your heart? What is one small sin you tolerate that you now see could be a stepping stone to greater disobedience?
?
The passage says that obedience is the fruit of faith and certainty. If your faith is a tree, what kind of fruit is it producing right now? Are your deeds aligned with the level of certainty you claim to have, or is there a gap that reveals a weakness in your yaqin?
?
Consider the analogy of sight: you have the root of faith, but how sharp is your spiritual vision? Are you seeing the truth clearly, or are you like one who sees only the thick lines of religion while missing the subtle details? What would it take to sharpen your vision through knowledge and remembrance?

Differences in the Strength of Faith
Addresses the ruling on comparing faith and the variation in its light.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ruling against claiming equality with prophets or angels in faith, and how the light of tawhid varies in hearts, as illustrated by the analogy of lights and supporting hadith.

Core Idea
The light of faith in hearts varies from the sun to a weak lamp; only God knows its full range.

After establishing equality in the essence of faith, the author now addresses the immense diversity in its strength. He cites the ruling of Imam Muhammad against claiming equality with Gabriel, and paints a vivid picture of the varying lights of tawhid in hearts, supported by two prophetic hadith.

Linguistic Definitions of Iman and Islam
The commentator defines Iman and Islam linguistically with Quranic and hadith evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic meanings of Iman and Islam, their relationship, and the Ahl al-Sunna position that actions are distinct from Iman.

Core Idea
Iman is inward affirmation; Islam is outward submission—both are distinct yet inseparable.

This lesson explores the linguistic roots of Iman and Islam. The commentator explains that Iman means 'tasdiq' (affirmation) while Islam means 'submission and compliance.' He then clarifies the Sharia relationship: actions are not part of Iman but are distinct, contra the Mu'tazila. Key Quranic verses and the hadith of Jibril support these definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the commentator's structure: first statement, then distinction, then evidence.
2
Understand the linguistic definitions
Note that Iman = tasdiq (affirmation) and Islam = submission (taslim/inqiyad).
Look for the Quranic verses that prove these definitions.
3
Identify the key evidence
Find the Quranic verses for each definition and the rule of the conjunctive 'wa'.
The verse from Yusuf shows Iman as affirmation; Al 'Imran shows Islam as submission.
4
Grasp the theological distinction
Recognize that actions are distinct from Iman, proven by the conjunction 'wa' and the Bedouins verse.
This refutes the Mu'tazila who consider actions as part of Iman.
5
Reflect on the hadith of Jibril
See how it reinforces the distinction: Iman is tasdiq, Islam is performance.
The hadith shows a clear division between inward belief and outward action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإسلام هو التسليم والانقياد لأوامر الله تعالى ففي طريق اللغة فرق بين الإيمان والإسلام ولكن لا يكون إيمان بلا إسلام ولا إسلام بلا إيمان وحاصله أن العمل مغاير للإيمان عند أهل السنة والجماعة لا أنه جزء منه وركن له من الأركان كما يقوله المعتزلة، لما يدل عليه العطف الذي هو في الأصل مغايرة بين المعطوف والمعطوف عليه، حيث جاء في القرآن من نحو قوله تعالى: ﴿آمَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا﴾ [البقرة:25]. والإسلام هو التسليم أي باطنا والانقياد لأوامر الله تعالى أي ظاهرا، ففي طريق اللغة وفي نسخة: من طريق اللغة فرق بين الإيمان والإسلام فإن الإيمان في اللغة التصديق كما قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا أَنْتَ بِمُؤْمِنٍ لَنَا﴾ [يوسف:17]، أي بمصدق لنا في هذه القصة، والإسلام مطلق الانقياد ومنه قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَهُ أَسْلَمَ مَنْ فِي السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ طَوْعًا وَكَرْهًا﴾ [آل عمران:83]، أي الملائكة والمسلمون طوعا والكفرة كرها حين اليأس، فالإيمان مختص بالانقياد الباطني والإسلام مختص بالانقياد الظاهري كما يشير إليه قوله تعالى: ﴿قَالَتِ الْأَعْرَابُ آمَنَّا قُلْ لَمْ تُؤْمِنُوا وَلَكِنْ قُولُوا أَسْلَمْنَا وَلَمَّا يَدْخُلِ الْإِيمَانُ فِي قُلُوبِكُمْ﴾ [الحجرات:14]، وكما يدل عليه حديث جبرائيل عليه السلام حيث فرق بين الإيمان والإسلام بأن جعل الإيمان محض التصديق والإسلام هو القيام بالإقرار وعمل الأبرار في مقام التوفيق.
And Islam is submission and compliance to the commands of Allah the Exalted. In the way of language there is a distinction between Iman and Islam, but there cannot be Iman without Islam nor Islam without Iman. The summary is that action is distinct from Iman according to Ahl al-Sunna wal-Jama'a, not that it is a part of it and a pillar of its pillars as the Mu'tazila say, because of what the conjunctive particle indicates, which in origin implies distinction between the conjunct and the conjoined, as occurs in the Qur'an like His statement the Exalted: 'those who believed and did righteous deeds' [al-Baqarah 2:25]. And Islam is submission inwardly and compliance to the commands of Allah the Exalted outwardly. So in the way of language—and in a copy: from the way of language—there is a distinction between Iman and Islam. For Iman in language is tasdiq (affirmation) as Allah the Exalted said: 'And you are not a believer (mu'min) to us' [Yusuf 12:17], i.e., not affirming us in this story. And Islam is absolute submission, and from it is His statement the Exalted: 'And to Him submits whoever is in the heavens and the earth willingly or unwillingly' [Al 'Imran 3:83], i.e., the angels and Muslims willingly and the disbelievers unwillingly at the time of despair. So Iman is specific to inward submission and Islam is specific to outward submission, as is indicated by His statement the Exalted: 'The Bedouins said: We believe. Say: You have not believed, but say: We have submitted, for faith has not yet entered your hearts' [al-Hujurat 49:14], and as is indicated by the hadith of Jibril peace be upon him, where he distinguished between Iman and Islam by making Iman pure tasdiq and Islam the performance of affirmation and the deeds of the righteous in the station of success.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage defines the linguistic meanings of Iman and Islam and then clarifies the Sharia relationship. The commentator begins by stating that Islam linguistically means submission and compliance to Allah's commands. He acknowledges that linguistically there is a distinction (farq) between Iman and Islam, but immediately adds the Sharia principle that one cannot exist without the other. He then summarizes the position of Ahl al-Sunna: actions are distinct from Iman, not a part of it or a pillar, contrary to the Mu'tazila. He supports this with the rule of the conjunctive particle 'wa' in Quranic phrases like 'those who believed and did righteous deeds' (amanoo wa 'amiloo), where the conjunction implies distinction between the two. Next, he provides linguistic definitions: Iman is tasdiq (affirmation/belief), citing the verse of Prophet Yusuf (Joseph) where the brothers say 'you are not mu'min to us' meaning not affirming. Islam is defined as absolute submission (mutlaq al-inqiyad), citing the verse about all creatures submitting to Allah willingly or unwillingly. From this, he concludes: Iman is inward submission (inqiyad batini) and Islam is outward submission (inqiyad zahiri). He further corroborates this with the Quranic verse about the Bedouins who were told to say 'we submitted' (aslamna) not 'we believed' because faith had not entered their hearts, and with the famous hadith of Jibril where Iman is defined as tasdiq and Islam as the performance of affirmation and righteous deeds.

Key Terms
إيمان
إسلام
تسليم
انقياد
تصديق
أهل السنة والجماعة
معتزلة
عطف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think actions are part of Iman.
Correction: The passage shows actions are distinct, as indicated by the conjunction 'wa' and the Bedouins verse.

Checkpoint
What is the linguistic meaning of 'Iman' according to the passage?
A) Submission
B) Affirmation (tasdiq)
C) Outward compliance
D) Actions

Key Takeaway
Iman linguistically means affirmation (tasdiq) and is inward submission, while Islam means outward submission; they are distinct yet inseparable, and actions are not a part of Iman.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I believe', does your inward affirmation truly shape your outward actions, or are they disconnected? What evidence from your daily life shows that your heart submits to Allah?
?
The Bedouins were told they had only submitted outwardly, not believed inwardly. Are there areas in your life where you go through the motions of Islam without genuine faith in your heart? What would it take for faith to truly enter your heart?
?
The hadith of Jibril defines Iman as pure tasdiq. If your faith were tested today, would your actions bear witness that you truly affirm Allah and His Messenger, or would they reveal a gap? What one step can you take today to close that gap?

The Inseparability of Iman and Islam
The commentator explains the Sharia rule and analogy of back and belly.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Iman and Islam are inseparable in Islamic law, and how the commentator uses examples and an analogy to explain their relationship.

Core Idea
Iman and Islam are like the back and belly — neither exists without the other.

This passage from the commentary on Fiqh al-Akbar clarifies a crucial point: in Sharia, true faith (Iman) cannot exist without outward submission (Islam), nor can outward submission be valid without inward faith. The commentator provides examples from the People of the Book, Abu Talib, and Iblis, and then offers a vivid analogy to help us grasp this inseparable bond.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the contrary statements: 'no Iman without Islam' and 'no Islam without Iman'.
2
Identify the examples
Look for the three examples given for Iman without Islam: People of the Book, Abu Talib, Iblis. Ask why each example fits.
3
Understand the temporal reversal
The commentator notes that either can precede the other in time, but they are ultimately inseparable in essence.
This reinforces the idea that both are required for valid faith.
4
Reflect on the analogy
Consider the analogy of back and belly. Why is this a powerful illustration for the abstract concept?
The analogy makes the invisible relationship tangible.
5
Apply the conclusion
The passage concludes that Iman is in the heart, Islam in heart and body. Think about how this affects our understanding of faith and action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولكن لا يكون إيمان بلا إسلام أي انقياد باطني بلا انقياد ظاهري، كما كان لأهل الكتاب وكما وجد لأبي طالب حال الخطاب، وكما صدر لإبليس حال العتاب فلابد من جمعها في صوب الصواب. ولا إسلام بلا إيمان تأكيد لما قبله وإشارة إلى أنه يستوي تقدم الإسلام على تحقق الإيمان وعكسه في مقام الإيقان، إذ ربما يتقدم التصديق الباطني ويتأخر الانقياد الظاهري كمؤمني أهل الكتاب، وربما يتقدم الإسلام ظاهراً ثم يوجد التصديق باطنا كما وقع لبعض المنافقين حيث سلكوا في الآخر طريق المؤمنين، ولعل هذا وجه الحكمة في قضية المؤلفة. فهما أي الإسلام والإيمان كشيء واحد حيث هما لا ينفكان كالظهر مع البطن أي للإنسان، فإنه لا يتحقق وجود أحدهما بدون الآخر وهذا تمثيل للمعقول بالمحسوس فتدبر، وقد ورد الإسلام علانية والإيمان سرا، أي مبني على نيته والحاصل أن الإيمان محله القلب والإسلام موضعه القلب والجسد الكامل منهما يتركب.
But there cannot be Iman without Islam, i.e., inward submission without outward submission, as was the case with the People of the Book and as occurred with Abu Talib at the time of address, and as issued from Iblis at the time of rebuke, so it is necessary to combine them in the direction of correctness. And there is no Islam without Iman, confirming the previous statement and indicating that the precedence of Islam over the realization of Iman and its opposite are equal in the station of certainty. For sometimes inward affirmation precedes and outward submission is delayed, as with the believers from the People of the Book, and sometimes outward Islam precedes and then inward affirmation occurs, as happened with some hypocrites who later followed the path of the believers. Perhaps this is the wisdom in the matter of those whose hearts are reconciled. So both of them, i.e., Islam and Iman, are like one thing, for they are inseparable like the back with the belly of a person. Indeed, the existence of one without the other is not realized. And this is an analogy of the conceptual to the tangible, so reflect. And it has been reported that Islam is public and Iman is secret, i.e., built on intention. The conclusion is that Iman's locus is the heart, and Islam's place is the heart and the body, and the complete person is composed of both.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the Sharia condition that Iman and Islam are inseparable. The commentator begins by asserting the negative: there cannot be Iman without Islam, meaning inward submission without outward compliance. He gives three examples of where inward submission existed without outward compliance: the People of the Book (who believed in God but did not follow Muhammad), Abu Talib (who supported the Prophet but did not openly embrace Islam), and Iblis (who acknowledged God but disobeyed). Thus, in Sharia, both are required. Conversely, there cannot be Islam without Iman, which is a corroborating statement. He then notes that temporally, either can precede the other: inward belief may come before outward action (as with believing Jews who later accepted Islam outwardly), or outward action may come before inward belief (as with some hypocrites who later became sincere). He suggests this is the wisdom behind the institution of 'those whose hearts are reconciled' (al-mu'allafatu qulubuhum). The key analogy follows: Iman and Islam are like the back and belly of a person—they cannot exist separately. This is a concrete analogy for an abstract concept. He concludes that Iman is located in the heart (mahalluhu al-qalb), while Islam pertains to both heart and body (mawdi'uhu al-qalb wa al-jasad). The complete human being (al-jasad al-kamil) is composed of both.

إبليس

الظهر
البطن
محل
موضع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Iman and Islam can be separated temporally in all cases, meaning that one can be valid without the other in some circumstances.
Correction: The commentator shows that while one may precede the other in time, they are inseparable in essence. Neither can be considered valid without the other in the Sharia ruling.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Consider your own life: is there an area where you believe something in your heart but fail to live it out? What is one specific outward action you will take today to align your Islam with your Iman?
?
Abu Talib supported the Prophet and believed in his message, yet he did not outwardly submit. Are there any comforts, statuses, or relationships that hold you back from fully submitting to Allah's commands? What would it cost you to surrender them completely?
?
The passage mentions that even hypocrites eventually became sincere believers after outwardly practicing Islam. Have you ever felt your actions were ahead of your heart? How can you use this insight to increase your sincerity without waiting for perfect conviction first?

The Comprehensive Meaning of Deen
Definition of Deen as encompassing Iman, Islam, and Sharia, with support from revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term 'Deen' in Islamic theology encompasses Iman, Islam, and all Sharia rulings, and that its foundational essence is tawhid, as affirmed by the Qur'an, the 'Aqida of al-Tahawi, and the prophetic hadith.

Core Idea
Deen is the single, balanced path of tawhid, encompassing all divine laws.

When we speak of 'religion' in Islam, are we referring only to belief, or also to practice? This lesson explores the comprehensive meaning of Deen as articulated by Imam Abu Hanifa and later scholars. We will see how Deen unites Iman, Islam, and Sharia into a single concept, and why it is described as a middle path between extremes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Notice how the commentator begins with a definition and then supports it with Quranic verses and other sources.
The Arabic uses technical terms like tasdiq and iqrar.
2
Identify the components of Deen
Look for what Deen includes: Iman, Islam, and all Sharia (rulings). Also note the Quranic evidence.
The verses are from Al Imran, al-Hajj, al-Ma'ida.
3
Clarify the Imam's intention
The commentator explicitly corrects a misinterpretation: Imam Abu Hanifa did not mean Deen applies to each component individually. Understand why this correction matters.
This guards against limiting Deen to only one aspect.
4
Explore the balanced nature of Deen from al-Tahawi
Read the excerpt from al-Tahawi's 'Aqida, which states Deen is one and balanced between six pairs of extremes.
These pairs include ghuluww vs taqsir, tashbih vs ta'til, etc.
5
Connect to the prophetic hadith
The hadith from Abu Huraira shows that all prophets shared one religion, i.e., tawhid. Reflect on how this unifies the message.
The hadith is from Sahih al-Bukhari.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدين اسم واقع على الإيمان والإسلام والشرائع كلها أي الأحكام جميعها، والمعنى أن الدين إذا أطلق فالمراد به التصديق والإقرار وقبول الأحكام للأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام كما من قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَنْ يَبْتَغِ غَيْرَ الْإِسْلَامِ دِينًا فَلَنْ يُقْبَلَ مِنْهُ﴾ [آل عمران:85]، وقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ الدِّينَ عِنْدَ اللهِ الْإِسْلَامُ﴾ [آل عمران:19]، وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا جَعَلَ عَلَيْكُمْ فِي الدِّينِ مِنْ حَرَجٍ﴾ [الحج:78]، وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَرَضِيتُ لَكُمُ الْإِسْلَامَ دِينًا﴾ [المائدة:3]، وليس مراد الإمام الأعظم أن الدين يطلق على كل واحد من الإيمان والإسلام والشرائع بانفرادها كما توهم شارح في المقام؛ لأنه خارج عن نظام المرام. وفي عقيدة الطحاوي ودين الله في الأرض والسماء واحد وهو بين الغلو والتقصير، وبين التشبيه والتعطيل وبين الجبر والقدر وبين الأمن واليأس. وفي الصحيح عن أبي هريرة رضي الله تعالى عنه مرفوعا: ((إنا معاشر الأنبياء ديننا واحد)) يعني أصله، وهو التوحيد وما يتعلق به. صحيح البخاري، كتاب أحاديث الأنبياء، باب واذكر في الكتاب ... إلخ، 458/2، حديث: 3443.
And Deen (religion) is a name applied to Iman, Islam, and all the Sharia, i.e., all the rulings. The meaning is that when 'Deen' is used absolutely, what is intended by it is affirmation (tasdiq), acknowledgment (iqrar), and acceptance of the rulings from the prophets (peace and blessings be upon them), as from His statement the Exalted: 'And whoever seeks a religion other than Islam, it will not be accepted from him' [Al 'Imran 3:85], and His statement: 'Indeed the religion with Allah is Islam' [Al 'Imran 3:19], and His statement: 'And He has not placed upon you in the religion any hardship' [al-Hajj 22:78], and His statement: 'And I have chosen for you Islam as a religion' [al-Ma'ida 5:3]. And it is not the intention of the Great Imam (Abu Hanifa) that 'Deen' is applied to each one of Iman, Islam, and Sharia individually, as a commentator imagined in this context, because that is outside the intended scheme. And in the 'Aqida of al-Tahawi: 'And the Deen of Allah in the earth and the heavens is one, and it is between excess and deficiency, between tashbih and ta'til, between jabr and qadar, and between security and despair.' And in the Sahih on the authority of Abu Huraira (may Allah be pleased with him) raised (marfu'an): 'Indeed we, the company of prophets, our religion is one.' Meaning its foundation, which is tawhid and what pertains to it. Sahih al-Bukhari, Book of Stories of the Prophets, Chapter 'And mention in the Book...' etc., 2/458, Hadith 3443.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage defines the comprehensive concept of Deen (religion). The commentator begins by stating that Deen is a name that covers Iman, Islam, and all Sharia rulings. When the term 'Deen' is used generally, it refers to tasdiq (affirmation), iqrar (acknowledgment), and acceptance of the prophets' rulings. He supports this with four Quranic verses: (1) 'Whoever seeks a religion other than Islam, it will not be accepted'; (2) 'Indeed the religion with Allah is Islam'; (3) 'He has not placed hardship in the religion'; (4) 'I have chosen Islam as a religion'. He then clarifies that Imam Abu Hanifa (al-Imam al-A'zam) did not intend that 'Deen' applies to each component (Iman, Islam, Sharia) individually, contrary to what some commentator imagined, because that would be outside the intended scope. The commentator then cites the 'Aqida of al-Tahawi, stating that Allah's religion in earth and heaven is one, and it is balanced between extremes: between ghuluww (excess) and taqsir (deficiency), between tashbih (anthropomorphism) and ta'til (denial of attributes), between jabr (compulsion) and qadar (free will), and between amn (security from God's punishment) and ya's (despair of God's mercy). Finally, he cites the sahih hadith from Abu Huraira that the prophets' religion is one, meaning their foundational principle is tawhid and what pertains to it.

Correction: Imam Abu Hanifa meant Deen encompasses all three together. Applying it individually is outside his intended scheme.

Islam and Iman: Inseparable Like Back and Belly
The author explains the inseparability of Islam and Iman with a body analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Islam and Iman are two inseparable dimensions of faith, analogous to the back and belly of a human body, and why this matters for theological completeness.

Core Idea
Islam and Iman are two sides of one reality—neither exists without the other.

This lesson explores a powerful analogy from the classical tradition: Islam and Iman are like the back and belly of a person. While they have distinct meanings—inner conviction and outward practice—they are fundamentally inseparable. The author uses this image to clarify a key point about the nature of faith, supported by examples from the early Muslim community.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation. Let the author's analogy and examples sink in before interpreting.
2
Understand the analogy
Focus on the statement 'فهما كالظهر مع البطن'. Ask yourself: what does the back and belly have in common? They are both parts of one body; one cannot exist without the other. Similarly, Islam and Iman are distinct but inseparable.
The author emphasizes they are 'like one thing' (كشيء واحد).
3
Examine the examples
The passage gives two examples: believers among the People of the Book (inward faith before outward submission) and some hypocrites (outward submission before inward faith). These show that one can precede the other in time, but ultimately both must be present for complete religion.
The reference to 'mu'allafatu qulubuhum' (those whose hearts are reconciled) hints at the wisdom in delaying inward faith for some.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes: Iman's place is the heart, Islam's place is the heart and body. The complete person is composed of both. This reinforces the inseparability.
Contrast with the mistaken view that one can have Iman without practice or practice without true faith.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهما كالظهر مع البطن والدين اسم واقع على الإيمان والإسلام والشرائع كلها الإيمان وعكسه في مقام الإيقان، إذ ربما يتقدم التصديق الباطني ويتأخر الانقياد الظاهري كمؤمني أهل الكتاب، وربما يتقدم الإسلام ظاهراً ثم يوجد التصديق باطنا كما وقع لبعض المنافقين حيث سلكوا في الآخر طريق المؤمنين، ولعل هذا وجه الحكمة في قضية المؤلفة. (فهما) أي الإسلام والإيمان كشيء واحد حيث هما لا ينفكان كالظهر) مع (البطن أي للإنسان، فإنه لا يتحقق وجود أحدهما بدون الآخر وهذا تمثيل للمعقول بالمحسوس فتدبر، وقد ورد الإسلام علانية والإيمان ،سرا، أي مبني على نيته والحاصل أن الإيمان محله القلب والإسلام موضعه القلب والجسد الكامل منهما يتركب.
So they two (Islam and Iman) are like the back with the belly. And 'din' is a name applied to Iman, Islam, and all the laws—Iman and its opposite in the station of certainty. For sometimes inward affirmation precedes and outward submission lags, like the believers among the People of the Book; and sometimes Islam appears outwardly, then affirmation is found inwardly, as occurred to some hypocrites who later followed the path of the believers. (So they two) i.e., Islam and Iman are like one thing, since they are inseparable, like the back with the belly of a human; indeed, the existence of one without the other is not realized, and this is a representation of the intellectual by the sensible, so reflect. And it has been reported that Islam is open and Iman is secret, i.e., built on intention. The conclusion is that Iman's place is the heart, and Islam's place is the heart and the body; the complete (person) is composed of both.

Commentary
This passage defines the relationship between Islam and Iman, arguing that they are inseparable, like the back and belly of a person. The author explains that although they are distinct in meaning—Iman being inner affirmation (tasdiq) and Islam being outer submission (inqiyad)—they cannot exist independently. He gives examples: some believers (like those from the People of the Book) may have inner faith before outward practice, and some hypocrites may outwardly practice Islam before inner faith develops. The analogy emphasizes that just as the back and belly are both essential parts of the body, Iman and Islam are both necessary for complete religion. The passage concludes by locating Iman in the heart and Islam in both heart and body, with the whole person being constituted by both.

Correction: The author explicitly states that Islam and Iman are inseparable, like back and belly; the complete person is composed of both. Neither exists without the other in a full sense.

Balancing Fear and Hope: A Quranic and Rational Obligation
Commentator explains obligation and mutual necessity of fear and hope, defining praiseworthy forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutual necessity of fear and hope in Islam and how to distinguish praiseworthy fear and hope from their blameworthy counterparts.

Core Idea
True fear and hope are balanced; without the other, they become despair or false security.

This lesson explores a foundational teaching from the classical text: faith requires both fear and hope in a balanced manner. The author grounds this in Quranic verses and rational analysis, then clarifies what constitutes genuine fear and hope versus dangerous extremes. You will learn to recognize the Islamic middle path between despair and complacency.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the author's structure: obligation, Quranic proof, rational interdependence, then definitions of praiseworthy and blameworthy states.
The passage uses a logical flow from general obligation to specific conditions.
2
Identify the Quranic evidence
Find the two verses cited (Az-Zumar 9 and As-Sajda 16) and note how they establish the command to have both fear and hope.
Both verses pair fear with hope or fear with hope in a worship context.
3
Understand the logical interdependence
The author argues that hope implies fear (otherwise it's false security) and fear implies hope (otherwise it's despair). See how this rational step (tahqīq) is crucial.
This reasoning prevents extremes.
4
Distinguish praiseworthy from blameworthy
Praiseworthy fear prevents sin; praiseworthy hope is based on obedience or repentance. Contrast with false hope without action.
The warning against delusion (ghurūr) and wishful thinking is key.
5
Reflect on personal application
Consider how this teaching balances your own spiritual state. Are you leaning toward despair or false hope? The middle path is the sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أنه يجب على العبد أن يكون خائفا راجيا لقوله تعالى: آمَنْ هُوَ قَانِتُ أَنَا الَّيْلِ سَاجِدًا وَقَا بِمَا يَحْذَرُ الْأَخِرَةَ وَيَرْجُوا رَ حَمَةَ رَي [الزمر: 9]، وقوله تعالى: يَدْعُونَ رَبَّهُمْ خَوْفًا وَطَمَعًا [السجدة : ١٦ ] . والتحقيق أن الرجاء يستلزم الخوف، ولولا ذلك لكان أمنا والخوف يستلزم الرجاء، ولولا ذلك لكان قنوطا ويأسا، فالخوف المحمود الصادق ما حال بين صاحبه وبين محارم الله سبحانه، فإذا تجاوز ذلك خيف منه اليأس والقنوط، والرجاء المحمود رجاء رجل عمل بطاعة الله تعالى على نور من ربه فهو راج لمثوبته، أو رجل أذنب ذنبا ثم تاب منه إلى الله فهو راج لمغفرته. أما إذا كان الرجل متماديا في التفريط والخطايا ويرجو رحمة الله تعالى بلا عمل، فهذا هو الغرور والتمني والرجاء الكاذب.
Know that it is incumbent upon the servant to be fearful and hopeful, due to the saying of the Most High: 'Is one who is devoutly obedient during the hours of the night, prostrating and standing, fearing the Hereafter and hoping for the mercy of his Lord?' [Az-Zumar: 9], and His saying: 'They call upon their Lord in fear and in hope' [As-Sajda: 16]. The verification is that hope necessitates fear; were it not so, it would be security (from God's plan), and fear necessitates hope; were it not so, it would be despair and hopelessness. Praiseworthy genuine fear is that which stands between its possessor and the prohibited things of God Almighty. If it exceeds that, then despair and hopelessness are feared from it. Praiseworthy hope is the hope of a man who performs obedience to God Almighty upon light from his Lord, so he hopes for His reward; or a man who commits a sin and then repents from it to God, so he hopes for His forgiveness. But if a man is persistent in neglect and sins and hopes for the mercy of God Almighty without (corresponding) action, then this is delusion, wishful thinking, and false hope.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a proof and definition. It begins by establishing the obligation of both fear and hope through two Quranic verses. Then, using rational analysis (tahqīq), the author demonstrates the interdependence of hope and fear: hope implies fear (otherwise it would be a false sense of security), and fear implies hope (otherwise it would be despair). The author then defines the praiseworthy form of each: fear that prevents sin, and hope that is based on obedient action or repentance. The false hope of the persistent sinner without action is condemned as delusion and wishful thinking.

Key Terms

الرجاء
القنوط
اليأس
الغرور
التمني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any amount of fear or hope is good; one can hope for God's mercy without changing one's sinful lifestyle.
Correction: The passage teaches that hope must be accompanied by obedient action or genuine repentance. Hope without action is false hope (ghurūr) and is blameworthy.

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on a person who persists in sin but hopes for God's mercy without repentance?
A) This is praiseworthy hope
B) This is delusion and false hope
C) This is the hope of the righteous
D) This is despair

Key Takeaway
Genuine fear and hope are mutually supportive and lead to righteous action; hope without action is delusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Think of a sin you repeated today or this week. Did you feel false hope that 'God will forgive anyway' without a concrete plan to change? What action can you take right now to turn that into true hope?
?
If you were to die tonight, would your fear of the Hereafter be genuine enough to have held you back from pleasure, or would your hope have been based on mere wishful thinking? Write one specific, measurable change you will make tomorrow to act on your hope.

The Wing Analogy and the Saying of 'Umar
The commentator quotes the wing analogy and 'Umar's statement on fear and hope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the wing analogy illustrates the complementary relationship between fear and hope in the believer's heart.

Core Idea
Fear and hope are like two wings: both needed for spiritual flight.

This lesson presents a profound analogy from Abu Ali al-Rudhbari that balances fear and hope in the spiritual life. It is then reinforced by the example of Caliph 'Umar, who embodied this balance. You will see why both emotions are essential and how they complement each other.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation without overanalyzing. Let the imagery of the bird and the wings sink in.
Notice that the analogy uses a familiar object to teach a spiritual truth.
2
Identify the three states of the wings
The analogy describes three conditions: balanced wings, one wing deficient, and both wings lost. For each, note what happens to the bird.
Each condition corresponds to a spiritual state of the believer.
3
Connect to 'Umar's saying
Read the quote from 'Umar. See how he expresses both hope and fear simultaneously. How does this mirror the balanced state of the wings?
Umar's saying provides a practical example from a revered companion.
4
Reflect on personal application
Consider your own spiritual state: do you tend more toward fear or hope? How can you strive for balance?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو علي الروذباري رحمه الله: «الخوف والرجاء كجناحي الطائر إذا استويا استوى الطير وتم طيرانه، وإذا نقص أحدهما وقع فيه النقص، وإذا ذهبا صار الطائر في حد الموت». وهذا الذي ذكره الشيخ موافق لما روي عن عمر رضي الله عنه أنه قال: «لو نودي في المحشر: إنّ واحدا يدخل الجنة لأرجو أن أكون أنا وإن قيل: إنّ واحدا يدخل النار أخاف أن أكون أنا.
Abu Ali al-Rudhbari, may God have mercy on him, said: 'Fear and hope are like the two wings of a bird. When they are balanced, the bird is balanced and its flight is complete; when one of them decreases, deficiency occurs in it; and when both disappear, the bird reaches the point of death.' And this which the shaykh mentioned is in agreement with what has been narrated from 'Umar, may God be pleased with him, that he said: 'If it were announced in the gathering [of Resurrection] that one person will enter Paradise, I would hope that I am that one; and if it were said that one person will enter the Fire, I would fear that I am that one.'
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents a simile and a historical example. Abu Ali al-Rudhbari compares fear and hope to a bird's two wings: both must be present and balanced for proper spiritual flight. Deficiency in either leads to imperfection; absence of both leads to spiritual death. The author then notes that this understanding aligns with the reported saying of 'Umar ibn al-Khattab, who expressed simultaneous hope and fear regarding his own fate in the Afterlife, showing the balanced state of the believer.

Key Terms

الرجاء
جناحي الطائر
المحشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think fear and hope are opposites that cannot coexist; one must choose one over the other.
Correction: The wing analogy shows they are complementary, like two wings of a bird; both are necessary for spiritual flight and balance.

Checkpoint

C) The bird becomes unbalanced
D) The bird's flight is complete

Key Takeaway
The believer must maintain a balance of fear and hope, as exemplified by the wing analogy and 'Umar's saying.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If your fear and hope were physical wings, would you be soaring, wobbling, or crashing? Commit to one tangible action you will take in the next 24 hours to restore balance—whether by remembering a verse of hope or a verse of warning.

Should Fear or Hope Dominate? Two Opinions
The commentator presents two views on which should prevail, with evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two scholarly opinions on the balance of fear and hope in one's spiritual life, and the evidence each opinion uses.

Core Idea
Both fear and hope have their place, but scholars differ on which should prevail and when.

The spiritual life requires a balance between fear of Allah's punishment and hope in His mercy. Scholars have discussed which of these two should dominate. This passage presents two views: one that hope should generally prevail, and another that distinguishes between different stages of life. Let's examine each opinion and its evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two opinions and the evidence cited for each.
2
Identify the first opinion
The first opinion says hope should be predominant. It cites a sacred hadith where Allah says He is as His servant thinks of Him.
3
Identify the second opinion
The second opinion says fear should dominate in youth and health, hope in old age and illness. It cites the Prophet's advice before his death.
4
Compare the evidence
Notice that the first opinion uses a general hadith, while the second uses a specific hadith about the time of death. Both are authentic.
5
Reflect on application
Think about how these opinions might affect one's spiritual practice. Which view seems more balanced?

وقال بعضهم: ينبغي أن يكون الرجاء غالبا للحديث القدسي: ((أنا عند ظن عبدي بي، فليظن بي ما شاء)). وقال بعضهم: الأولى أن يكون الخوف غالبا عند الشباب والصحة، والرجاء حال الكبر والمرض، لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام قبل موته بثلاث: ((لا يموتن أحدكم إلاّ وهو يحسن الظن بربه)).

And some said: It is more appropriate that fear be predominant in youth and health, and hope in old age and illness, due to his saying, peace be upon him, three days before his death: 'Let none of you die except while he has good opinion of his Lord.'
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents two opinions on the balance of fear and hope. The first, supported by a divine hadith (hadith qudsi), argues that hope should generally dominate because God responds to the servant's good opinion. The second opinion distinguishes based on life stages: fear should dominate in youth and health (to prevent sin), while hope should dominate in old age and illness (to encourage good opinion at death), citing the Prophet's advice shortly before his death. The author does not explicitly choose between them but records both views.

?
