Fleeing to God Through Fear
The commentator presents the paradoxical nature of fearing God and supports it with revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the unique nature of fear of God that drives one to seek refuge in Him, and the scriptural basis for this concept.

Core Idea
Fear of God leads not to escape from Him but to flight to Him.

This lesson explores a profound paradox in Islamic spirituality: while all other fears cause us to flee from the object of fear, fear of God draws us closer to Him. The commentator explains this using Quranic and Prophetic evidence, showing that the only true refuge is God Himself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the initial claim about fear of God compared to other fears.
The claim is that fear of God makes you flee to Him, not from Him.
2
Contrast normal fear with fear of God
Notice the contrast: 'everyone whom you fear, you flee from him, except God Almighty.' This is the key paradox.
3
Examine the scriptural proofs
Look at the Quranic verse (51:50) and the hadith, which support the claim that fleeing to God is the proper response.
The verse commands 'Flee to God'; the hadith says there is no refuge except in God.
4
Reflect on the spiritual lesson
Consider how this shapes the believer's relationship with God: fear should lead to closeness, not distance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا وكل أحد إذا خفته هربت منه إلا الله تعالى فإنك إذا خفته هربت إليه، فالخائف هارب من ربه إلى ربه كما يشير إليه قوله تعالى: فَفِرُّوا إِلَى اللهِ [الذاريات: ٥٠]، وقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: لا ملجأ ولا منجا منك إلا إليك. وقال بعضهم: «من عبد الله بالحب وحده فهو زنديق، ومن عبده بالخوف وحده فهو حروري، ومن عبده بالرجاء فهو مرجىء، ومن عبده بالحب والخوف والرجاء فهو مؤمن موحد»،
This is so: everyone whom you fear, you flee from him, except God Almighty: if you fear Him, you flee to Him. So the fearful one is fleeing from his Lord to his Lord, as is indicated by His saying: 'Flee to God' [Adh-Dhariyat: 50], and his saying, peace be upon him: 'There is no refuge and no deliverance from You except to You.' And some said: 'Whoever worships God with love alone is a zindīq; whoever worships Him with fear alone is a ḥarūrī; whoever worships Him with hope alone is a murjiʼ; and whoever worships Him with love, fear, and hope is a believing monotheist.'
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage first makes a remarkable point about the nature of fear of God: unlike all other fears that cause flight from the feared object, fear of God causes flight to Him. This is supported by the Qur'anic command to flee to God and the prophetic supplication that there is no refuge except in God. Then, the passage reports a classification of worshippers by their dominant motivation: those who love alone are called zindīq (heretical), those who fear alone are called ḥarūrī (extremist like the Kharijites), those who hope alone are called murjiʼ (who neglect works), while the true believing monotheist combines all three: love, fear, and hope.

Key Terms
الخوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) You flee from Him
B) You flee to Him
C) You become indifferent
D) You despair

Key Takeaway
Fear of God uniquely drives the believer not away from Him, but towards Him as the ultimate refuge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel afraid of Allah's punishment, does that fear draw you closer to Him in prayer and obedience, or does it make you avoid thinking about Him? What specific action will you take this week to turn that fear into a sincere plea for His mercy?
?
The Prophet taught us to say, 'There is no refuge from You except to You.' In what area of your life are you still seeking security in anything other than Allah—whether it be wealth, people, or your own efforts? What is one concrete step you will take today to make Allah your only refuge?
?
The verse 'Flee to God' is an urgent command. What is one fear you are currently running from—fear of failure, loss, or judgment—that you should instead bring directly to Allah? Write down that fear and, right now, turn it into a supplication, asking Him to be your shelter.

Three Types of Worship: Love, Fear, Hope
The commentator classifies worshippers by their dominant motivation and identifies the balanced believer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the balanced approach to worship that combines love, fear, and hope, and the consequences of imbalance.

Core Idea
True monotheism demands a balance of love, fear, and hope in worship.

This lesson presents a classic classification of worshippers based on their primary motivation. Some worship out of love only, others out of fear only, others out of hope only, but the true believer integrates all three. The commentator uses sectarian labels to warn against extremes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types of worshippers
Read the passage and list the four categories: worship by love alone, fear alone, hope alone, and all three combined.
2
Understand the sectarian labels
Each unbalanced type is associated with a sect: zindīq (love only), ḥarūrī (fear only), murjiʼ (hope only). Learn what these labels signify.
These labels are used to warn against heretical extremes.
3
Note the label for the balanced believer
The one who combines all three is called a 'believing monotheist' (muʼmin muwaḥḥid).
4
Reflect on the importance of balance
Consider why a single motivation is insufficient and how the combination reflects true Islamic spirituality.

Worshipping God for His Own Sake
Compilation of teachings on the motivation for worship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that true worship is motivated by God's intrinsic worthiness, not merely by fear of punishment or hope of reward, and that the highest form of worship is that of the 'free' worshippers.

Core Idea
God deserves worship for His own sake, not for what we gain.

This passage challenges a common understanding of Islamic worship. It presents a higher perspective, arguing that worship motivated solely by fear or hope is insufficient. The commentator quotes al-Razi, the Prophet, and Ali to illustrate that true believers worship out of gratitude and recognition of God's intrinsic worthiness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice the different authorities cited (al-Razi, the Prophet, Ali).
2
Identify al-Razi's key claim
What does al-Razi say about a person who worships only for fear of Hell or hope of Paradise? He says such a person is not a believer.
This is a strong statement that redefines the minimum of faith.
3
Examine the prophetic example
Why did the Prophet continue to worship intensely despite having his sins forgiven? He responded: 'Should I not be a grateful servant?'
Gratitude is a higher motivation than fear or hope.
4
Analyze Ali's three categories
Ali lists three levels: worship for reward (merchants), for fear (slaves), and for gratitude (free men). Understand each category and why the last is highest.
5
Reflect on the highest level
Consider what 'worship of the free' means. It is worship motivated by love and recognition of God's worthiness, not by personal gain.
This is the ideal the passage encourages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل قال المحقق الرازي: أما من لم يعبد الله إلا لخوف ناره أو طمع في جنته فليس بمؤمن؛ لأنه سبحانه يستحق أن يعبد ويطاع لذاته، وهذا معنى ما ورد: «نعم العبد صهيب لو لم يخف الله لم يعصه». ومن ثم لما قيل له صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم عند ما قام من الليل حتى تورمت قدماه: ((أتفعل هذا وقد غفر الله ذنبك ما تقدم وما تأخر؟ قال: أفلا أكون عبدا شكورا؟)) . (٤) وعن علي كرم الله وجهه: إن قوما عبدوا رغبة فتلك عبادة التجار، وإن قوما عبدوا رهبة فتلك عبادة العبيد، وإن قوما عبدوا شكرا فتلك عبادة الأحرار». كذا نقله عنه صاحب "ربيع الأبرار".
Rather, al-Muḥaqqiq al-Rāzī said: As for one who does not worship God except out of fear of His Fire or desire for His Garden, he is not a believer, because He, exalted is He, deserves to be worshipped and obeyed for His own essence. And this is the meaning of what has been reported: 'Excellent is the servant Ṣuhayb; if he did not fear God, he would not disobey Him.' And for that reason, when it was said to him, peace be upon him, when he stood at night until his feet became swollen: 'Do you do this when God has forgiven your past and future sins?' He said: 'Should I not be a grateful servant?' And from ʻAlī, may God honor his face: 'Indeed, some people worship out of desire, that is the worship of merchants; some people worship out of fear, that is the worship of slaves; and some people worship out of gratitude, that is the worship of free men.' Thus it is narrated from him by the author of 'Rabīʻ al-Abrār'.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents a higher perspective on the motivation for worship. Al-Rāzī is quoted as saying that a person who worships God only for fear of Hell or hope of Paradise is not a true believer, because God deserves worship for His own sake. This is supported by the saying about Ṣuhayb, indicating that his obedience is not merely from fear. The example of the Prophet is given: despite forgiveness, he worshipped out of gratitude. Finally, ʻAlī classifies worshippers into three levels: worship for reward (merchants), worship for fear (slaves), and worship out of gratitude (free men). The highest is worship motivated by God's intrinsic worthiness.

Key Terms
الخوف
الرجاء
الشكر
عبادة الأحرار
المحقق الرازي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Worshipping God out of fear of Hell or hope of Paradise is sufficient for true faith.
Correction: True faith requires worship motivated by God's intrinsic worthiness, love, and gratitude, as stated by al-Razi and Ali.

Checkpoint
According to al-Rāzī, what is the status of a person who worships God only for fear of Hell or hope of Paradise?
A) He is a good believer
B) He is not a believer
C) He is a hypocrite
D) He is a sinner

Key Takeaway
True believers worship God because He is worthy, not merely for reward or fear.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about your last prayer. Was it motivated more by the thought of avoiding Hell or by the awareness that Allah is worthy of your worship? If it was the former, what would it take to shift your heart to the latter?
?
Imagine all fear of punishment and hope for reward were removed from your heart tomorrow. Would you still pray, give charity, and seek Allah? If not, what does that reveal about the foundation of your faith?
?
Ali said the best worship is that of 'free men' — those who worship out of gratitude. What is one concrete way you can cultivate gratitude in your worship starting this week?

Faith and Disbelief

The Balanced Worship: Fear, Hope, and Love
The commentator presents the four types of worship based on motives and condemns extremes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why worship requires a balanced combination of fear, hope, and love, and why focusing on only one is considered deviant.

Core Idea
True worship balances fear, hope, and love; imbalance leads to heresy or extremism.

This passage presents a key classification in Islamic theology: the motives behind worship. It warns against focusing solely on love, fear, or hope, and declares that a true monotheist combines all three. The lesson explains these categories and their significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three categories
Read the Arabic passage once. Then look for the four categories of worshipers mentioned: zindīq, Ḥarūrī, Murjiʾ, and muʾmin muwaḥḥid. Notice how each is defined.
The categories are based on the dominant motive: love, fear, hope, or all three combined.
2
Understand the unique nature of fearing Allah
The passage explains that normally fear makes you flee from what you fear, but fearing Allah makes you flee to Him. This sets the foundation for why all three motives are needed.
This is supported by Quran 51:50 and a hadith.
3
Grasp the balance in different life stages
Some scholars recommend that fear be dominant in youth and health, while hope dominates in old age and illness. This shows the dynamic application of fear and hope.
4
Reflect on the consequence of imbalance
Ask yourself: why would worship by love alone be heretical (zindīq), by fear alone be Kharijite (Ḥarūrī), and by hope alone be Murjiʾ? The commentary will clarify.
The balanced believer avoids these extremes.
5
Apply the lesson to your own worship
Consider how you personally balance fear, hope, and love in your worship. The goal is to be a muʾmin muwaḥḥid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال بعضهم: الأولى أن يكون الخوف غالبا عند الشباب والصحة، والرجاء حال الكبر والمرض، لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام قبل موته بثلاث: ((لا يموتن أحدكم إلاّ وهو يحسن الظن بربه. هذا وكل أحد إذا خفته هربت منه إلا الله تعالى فإنك إذا خفته هربت إليه، فالخائف هارب من ربه إلى ربه كما يشير إليه قوله تعالى: ﴿فَفِرُّوا إِلَى اللهِ ﴾ [الذاريات: ٥٠]، وقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: (لا) ملجأ ولا منجا منك إلا إليك)) . (٣) وقال بعضهم: «من عبد الله بالحب وحده فهو زنديق، ومن عبده بالخوف وحده فهو حروري، ومن عبده بالرجاء فهو مرجىء، ومن عبده بالحب والخوف والرجاء فهو مؤمن موحد»، وأما كلام صاحب المنازل أن الرجاء أضعف منازل المريد فهو بالإضافة إلى مقام الحب الذي هو حال المريد، بل قال المحقق الرازي: أما من لم يعبد الله إلا لخوف ناره أو طمع في جنته فليس بمؤمن؛ لأنه سبحانه يستحق أن يعبد ويطاع لذاته، وهذا معنى ما ورد: «نعم العبد صهيب لو لم يخف الله لم يعصه». ومن ثم لما قيل له صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم عند ما قام من الليل حتى تورمت قدماه: ((أتفعل هذا وقد غفر الله ذنبك ما تقدم وما تأخر؟ قال: أفلا أكون عبدا شكورا؟)) . (٤) وعن علي كرم الله وجهه: إن قوما عبدوا رغبة فتلك عبادة التجار، وإن قوما عبدوا رهبة فتلك عبادة العبيد، وإن قوما عبدوا شكرا فتلك عبادة الأحرار». كذا نقله عنه صاحب "ربيع الأبرار".
And some of them said: It is more proper that fear be predominant in youth and health, and hope in old age and illness, due to his saying, peace and blessings be upon him, three days before his death: 'Let none of you die except while having good opinion of his Lord.' This: And every person, if you fear him, you flee from him, except Allah the Exalted, for if you fear Him, you flee to Him. Thus the fearful one flees from his Lord to his Lord, as is indicated by His saying, the Exalted: 'Flee to Allah' [Q 51:50], and his saying, peace and blessings be upon him: 'There is no refuge and no escape from You except to You.' And some of them said: 'Whoever worships Allah by love alone is a zindīq; whoever worships Him by fear alone is a Ḥarūrī; whoever worships Him by hope alone is a Murjiʾ; and whoever worships Him by love, fear, and hope is a believing monotheist.' As for the statement of the author of al-Manāzil that hope is the weakest of the stations of the seeker, it is relative to the station of love which is the state of the seeker. Rather, al-Muḥaqqiq al-Rāzī said: 'Whoever does not worship Allah except out of fear of His Fire or desire for His Paradise is not a believer, because He, glorified, deserves to be worshipped and obeyed for His own essence.' And this is the meaning of what has been related: 'Excellent is the servant Ṣuhayb; if he did not fear Allah, he would not disobey Him.' Moreover, when it was said to him, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, when he stood in prayer at night until his feet swelled: 'Do you do this when Allah has forgiven your past and future sins?' He said: 'Should I not be a grateful servant?' And from ʿAlī, may Allah ennoble his face: 'Indeed, a people worshipped out of desire—that is the worship of merchants; a people worshipped out of fear—that is the worship of slaves; a people worshipped out of gratitude—that is the worship of free men.' Thus reported from him by the author of Rabīʿ al-Abrār.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a classification of types of worship based on the motives of fear, hope, and love, with supporting arguments. It begins by noting that some scholars recommend that fear be dominant in youth and hope in old age, citing the hadith about having good opinion of Allah at death. Then it makes a key observation: normally, fearing someone makes you flee from them, but fearing Allah makes you flee to Him. This is supported by Quranic verse (51:50) and a hadith. The main classification comes next: 'Whoever worships Allah by love alone is a zindīq (heretic); by fear alone is a Ḥarūrī (Kharijite); by hope alone is a Murjiʾ; and by all three is a believing monotheist.' This shows that all three motives are necessary in balance. The passage then critiques a statement from the author of Manāzil that hope is the weakest station, explaining that this is relative to the station of love. Al-Rāzī is quoted to say that those who worship only for fear or hope are not true believers, because Allah deserves worship for His own essence. The hadith of Ṣuhayb and the Prophet's night prayer illustrate worship out of gratitude. Finally, a statement from ʿAlī classifies worship into that of merchants (desire), slaves (fear), and free men (gratitude).

Key Terms
الخوف (al-khawf)
الرجاء (al-rajāʾ)
الحب (al-ḥubb)
زنديق (zindīq)
حروري (ḥarūrī)
مرجىء (murjiʾ)
مؤمن موحد (muʾmin muwaḥḥid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Worshipping Allah out of love alone is sufficient and even superior.
Correction: The passage declares that worshipping by love alone is heretical (zindīq). True worship requires balancing love with fear and hope.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of one who worships Allah by love alone?
A) He is a true monotheist.
B) He is a zindīq (heretic).
C) He is a Ḥarūrī (Kharijite).
D) He is a Murjiʾ.

Key Takeaway
True worship combines fear, hope, and love; each alone leads to deviation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your worship, which motive dominates: fear of punishment, hope for reward, or love of Allah? How does that imbalance affect your relationship with Him? This week, commit to praying one extra rak'ah purely out of love.
?
The Prophet advised to have good opinion of Allah at death. Is your current state of hope strong enough to sustain you in your final moments? What fear or doubt about Allah's mercy lingers in your heart?
?
If you were asked to describe your worship using the four categories in the passage, which one would you fall into? What practical steps can you take today to bring love, fear, and hope into balance in your daily prayers and supplications?

Worship for Allah's Essence, Not for Reward or Punishment
The commentator argues that true worship is for Allah's essence, citing al-Razi, prophetic traditions, and Ali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the highest form of worship is motivated by gratitude and love for Allah's essence, not merely fear of hell or hope of paradise.

Core Idea
Worship Allah because He deserves it, not just out of desire for reward or fear of punishment.

After establishing the need for balance, the passage elevates the discussion to the ultimate motive: Allah's worthiness of worship. It quotes al-Razi, the hadith of the Prophet's night prayer, and Ali's classification to show that the highest worship is that of free people who worship out of gratitude.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the critique
Read the Arabic passage. Notice that it begins by addressing a statement that hope is the weakest station. The author reframes it as relative to the station of love.
2
Understand al-Razi's criterion
Al-Razi says that worshipping only out of fear or hope does not make one a true believer. Why? Because Allah deserves worship for His own essence.
This is a higher level of worship.
3
Analyze the examples
The Prophet's statement about Ṣuhayb and his own night prayer demonstrate worship out of fear of displeasing Allah and out of gratitude. These go beyond mere fear of hell or hope of paradise.
Ṣuhayb's fear was of disobedience, not of punishment.
4
Reflect on Ali's classification
Ali describes three types: worship for desire (merchants), worship for fear (slaves), and worship for gratitude (free men). Which type do you think is the highest?
The passage emphasizes that gratitude-based worship is the worship of free people.
5
Connect to the previous lesson
This lesson builds on the balanced approach. Now we see that the ultimate goal is to worship Allah for His own sake, out of love and gratitude.
This perfects the concept of worship.

?

?

?

Faith and Disbelief
beginner
5 min
Faith is One, Excellence Lies in Piety
The commentator defines faith, explains variation among believers, and cites al-Ṭaḥāwī.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that faith is a single reality of inner certainty, while believers differ in their actions, piety, and spiritual stations.

Core Idea
Faith is one; excellence lies in piety.

This passage from a classical commentary on Fiqh al-Akbar addresses a subtle but important question: Are all believers equal in faith? The commentator clarifies that faith itself is a single, unchanging reality—certainty in Allah's essence and attributes—but believers vary in their practice, piety, and spiritual states. This distinction, grounded in the words of Imam al-Ṭaḥāwī, shapes a balanced understanding of Islamic orthodoxy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation to grasp the core distinction between the unity of faith and the diversity of believers.
Pay attention to the definition of faith as 'certainty' (īqān).
2
Identify the key claim
Notice that faith is defined as certainty of Allah's essence and attributes. Variation among believers occurs only 'in what is below faith'—i.e., in actions, not in the core belief itself.
3
Understand al-Ṭaḥāwī's statement
Al-Ṭaḥāwī famously said: 'Faith is one, and its people are equal in its origin; but differentiation is by piety, mindfulness, opposing desire, and adhering to the primary [obligations].' This is the key proof-text.
Piety (khashyah) and mindfulness (taqwā) are the measures of excellence.
4
Note the commentator's refinement
The commentator prefers an interpretation that includes spiritual stations, not just obligations. He cites Sufi teachings to show that variation also applies to inner states and high ranks.
This aligns with the broader spiritual tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإيمان أي الإيقان بثبوت ذاته وتحقق صفاته، وهو معطوف على قوله والرجاء، ويتفاوتون أي المؤمنون فيما دون الإيمان أي في غير التصديق والإقرار بحسب تفاوت الأبرار في القيام بالأركان واختلاف الفجار في مراتب العصيان و في ذلك كله أي ويتفاوتون المقامات العلية والحالات السنية لاختلاف منازل الصوفية رحمهم الله تعالى أيضاً فيما ذكر من قال الطحاوي رحمه الله تعالى والإيمان واحد وأهله في أصله سواء، والتفاضل بالخشية والتقى : ومخالفة الهوى وملازمة الأولى. هذا وذهب شارح في هذا المقام إلى أن تقدير الكلام استواء أهل الإسلام في كونهم مكلّفين بهذه الأحكام، ولا يخفى أن ما اخترناه أدق في نظام المرام. ثم تحقيق هذه المقامات العلية محل بسطها كتب السادة الصوفية وقد بيّنا طرفا منها في التفسير والشروح الحديثية.
And faith (al-īmān): that is, certainty of the existence of His essence and the realization of His attributes. It is conjuncted to His saying 'and hope'. And 'they vary' (yatāfawatūna): that is, the believers, in what is below faith, i.e., in other than attestation and affirmation, according to the variation of the pious in performing the pillars and the difference of the wicked in degrees of disobedience. And 'in all of that': i.e., they vary in the high stations and elevated states due to the difference of the stations of the Sufis, may Allah have mercy on them. Also, among what has been mentioned, al-Ṭaḥāwī, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'Faith is one, and its people are equal in its origin; but differentiation is by piety (al-khashyah), mindfulness (al-taqwā), opposing desire, and adhering to the primary [obligations].' This: And one commentator opined in this context that the intended meaning of the statement is the equality of the people of Islam in their being obligated by these rulings. It is not hidden that what we have chosen is more precise in the order of the objective. Then, the realization of these high stations is the place for their elaboration in the books of the Sufi masters, and we have indicated some of them in the tafsīr and hadith commentaries.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage defines faith (īmān) as certainty of the existence of Allah's essence and the realization of His attributes. It is connected to the previous discussion of hope. Then it explains the phrase 'they vary' from the original text, meaning that believers differ in matters other than faith itself (i.e., in actions and states). The variation is according to the pious fulfilling the pillars of Islam and the wicked committing sins. The 'in all of that' refers to variation in spiritual stations and states, based on the different levels of the Sufis. The commentator cites al-Ṭaḥāwī who famously stated that faith is one and its people are equal in its origin, but they differ in piety, mindfulness, opposing desire, and adhering to what is best. The commentator then notes that another interpreter understood the text as meaning equality only in being obligated by rulings, but the commentator asserts his own interpretation is more precise. Finally, he remarks that detailed discussion of spiritual stations belongs in Sufi books, and he has touched on them in his other works.

Key Terms
الإيمان (al-īmān)
الإيقان (al-īqān)
التصديق (al-taṣdīq)
الإقرار (al-iqrār)
الأبرار (al-abrār)
الفجار (al-fujjār)
الخشية (al-khashyah)
التقوى (al-taqwā)
الطحاوي (al-Ṭaḥāwī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All believers are completely equal in faith and practice; there is no distinction among them.
Correction: Believers are equal in the root of faith (attestation and affirmation) but differ in actions, piety, and spiritual stations, as al-Ṭaḥāwī stated.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ṭaḥāwī as quoted in this passage, what is the basis for differentiation among people of faith?
A) Their essence of faith is different.
B) They vary in attestation and affirmation.
C) They differ in piety, mindfulness, opposing desire, and adhering to the primary.
D) They are equal in all aspects.

Key Takeaway
Faith is one in its essence; believers vary only in piety, practice, and spiritual rank.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that your core faith is equal to that of the most pious — but your station in the hereafter depends on your khashyah and taqwā. Are you content with merely 'having faith,' or are you actively opposing your desires and clinging to obligations to rise in maqāmāt? What specific desire are you still indulging that you know holds you back?
?
Al-Ṭaḥāwī says differentiation is by piety, opposing desire, and adhering to obligations. Examine your daily routine: where are you neglecting obligations or failing to oppose a desire? If you died tonight, would you be among the abrār or fujjār — not in faith, but in practice? What one change can you make today to tip the scale?
?
The commentator mentions 'high stations' (maqāmāt) of the Sufis. Do you ever feel spiritually stagnant? Could it be that you have mistaken a static faith for dynamic piety? Ask yourself: am I growing in khashyah and taqwā, or am I comfortable in a plateau? What is the next step Allah is calling you to — a neglected obligation, a desire to abandon, or a deeper mindfulness of Him?

Faith of the One Unreached by the Call
Defines and discusses the faith of those not reached by the call.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Hanafi position on the faith of those who have not received the prophetic call, and how it is analogous to the faith of a child before puberty.

Core Idea
The faith of the unreached is valid, like the faith of a discerning child.

This lesson explores a nuanced question in Islamic theology: what is the status of a person who has never encountered the message of Islam? The Hanafi school holds that such a person's faith is accepted, drawing an analogy with a child's faith before puberty. The passage from Sanusi's commentary presents this view with evidence from the Prophet's call to Ali and the gradual nature of Islamic obligations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation to grasp the main argument.
Pay attention to the author's condensed style.
2
Identify the Hanafi position
Note that the author states the Hanafi view as preponderant, contrary to the three other imams.
The phrase 'خلافا للأئمة الثلاثة' signals the disagreement.
3
Examine the evidence
The evidence includes the Prophet calling Ali to Islam and his response, plus consensus on validity of pre-puberty worship.
This parallels the condition of the one not reached by the call.
4
Consider the gradual obligation principle
The author links the discussion to the gradual introduction of duties, starting with tawhid.
This explains why obligations were not imposed from the start.
5
Reflect on implications
Think about how this view affects the theology of responsibility and divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صور عباداته من إيمان من لم تبلغه دعوة كإيمان الصبي عندهم، أي على القول المرجح من مذهبهم خلافا للأئمة الثلاثة؛ لأن النبي صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم دعا عليا إلى الإسلام فأجابه، مع الإجماع على أن صلاة وصوم ونحوهما صحيحة. وأما ما نقله البيهقي من أن الأحكام إنما علقت بالبلوغ بعد الهجرة عام الخندق وأما قبل ذلك فكانت منوطة بالتمييز، فيحتاج إلى بيان ذلك وكيفية وقوعه هنالك على أن أمور الإسلام في تكاليف الأحكام كانت تدريجية من الأهون إلى الأصعب لا بالعكس، ولذا كان التكليف أولا بالتوحيد، ثم زيد الصلاة والزكاة ونحوهما كما هو مقتضى حكمة الحكيم المجيد.
Among the forms of his acts of worship is the faith of one whom the call has not reached, like the faith of a child according to them, that is, according to the preponderant view in their school, contrary to the three imams. Because the Prophet (peace and blessings of God be upon him) called ʿAlī to Islam and he responded, with consensus that prayer, fasting, and similar acts are valid. As for what al-Bayhaqī narrated— that rulings were attached to puberty only after the migration in the year of the Trench, and before that they were dependent on discernment— this requires clarification of that and how it occurred there, given that the affairs of Islam in the obligations of rulings were gradual from the easier to the harder, not vice versa. Therefore, obligation was first with tawḥīd (monotheism), then prayer, zakāt, and similar acts were added, as required by the wisdom of the Most Wise, the Glorious.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the faith of the person who has never received the prophetic call. It states that according to the Ḥanafī school, such a person's faith is like that of a child, i.e., considered valid and accepted, contrary to the other three imams. The reasoning given is that the Prophet (ﷺ) called ʿAlī to Islam directly, and ʿAlī responded with faith, while consensus holds that the acts of worship (like prayer, fasting) performed before puberty are valid. The author then addresses a narration from al-Bayhaqī that rulings were tied to puberty only after the Hijra, indicating that before that they were tied to discernment (tamyīz). The author notes that this needs explanation, but the overall principle is that Islamic obligations were introduced gradually from the easy to the difficult, not the opposite. The first obligation was tawḥīd, then prayer, zakāt, etc., which is consistent with divine wisdom.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ḥanafī position equates the faith of the un-called with the faith of a child without basis.
Correction: The Hanafi position is based on the precedent of ʿAlī's early acceptance of the call and the consensus on the validity of pre-puberty worship.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the view that the one who has not been reached by the call has valid faith?
A) The universal consensus of the Companions.
B) The Prophet's direct call to ʿAlī and his acceptance, plus the validity of pre-puberty worship.
C) The Qur'anic verse about not burdening a soul beyond its capacity.
D) The fact that all humans are born with faith.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the faith of a person who has not been reached by the prophetic call is valid and acceptable, analogous to a child's faith before puberty, based on the precedent of Ali and the gradual revelation of obligations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Prophet accepted Ali's faith the moment he called him. If Allah sent a prophet to you today, would your heart respond immediately with full conviction in tawhid, or would you hesitate with excuses? What does your answer reveal about the strength of your faith right now?
?
The Hanafis teach that the faith of a child is valid and accepted. When did you first affirm belief in Allah? Do you treat that early faith as real and precious, or have you dismissed it as 'childish' and incomplete? How might acknowledging its validity change your relationship with your Creator?
?
Obligations were introduced gradually: first tawhid, then prayer, then zakat. Are you still struggling with the first step—pure monotheism—while pretending the later steps are enough? What hidden shirk or distraction needs to be repented of today?

The Prophet's Parents and al-Qari's Retraction
Affirms the parents died on disbelief and notes al-Qari's later retraction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the controversial issue of the Prophet's parents' faith and the later retraction of Mulla Ali al-Qari on this matter.

Core Idea
Even great scholars can change their positions; al-Qari's retraction shows the issue is not clear-cut.

This passage from a commentary on the Fiqh al-Akbar addresses a sensitive historical-theological debate: whether the Prophet's parents died upon disbelief or faith. The author takes a firm stance, but also records that the prominent Hanafi scholar Mulla Ali al-Qari later retracted his earlier view and adopted the position of al-Suyuti. This lesson unpacks the arguments and the significance of al-Qari's retraction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to get the full meaning.
Notice the strong language: 'mata 'ala al-kufr' and the comparison to deviants.
2
Identify the author's main claim
What is the author asserting about the Prophet's parents? How does he support his claim?
He says they died on disbelief and wrote a treatise refuting al-Suyuti.
3
Understand the comparison to deviant figures
Why does the author compare the objecting Hanafis to Jahm, Ibn Abi Dawud, and the Rafidah?
He sees their objection as a form of religious innovation or extremism.
4
Consider al-Qari's retraction
What did al-Qari say in Sharh al-Shifa? How does this change the picture?
Al-Qari shifted to the view that the parents were Muslims, following al-Suyuti.
5
Reflect on the lesson's core idea
Think about how this passage illustrates that scholarly opinions can evolve, even on sensitive topics.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن في بعض نسخ هذا الشرح زيدت هذه العبارة: [ووالدا رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ماتا على الكفر. وهذا رد على من قال إنهما ماتا على الإيمان أو ماتا على الكفر ثم أحياهما الله فماتا في مقام الإيمان. وقد أفردت لهذه المسألة رسالة مستقلة ودفعت ما ذكره السيوطي في رسائله الثلاث في تقوية هذه المقالة بالأدلة الجامعة المجتمع من الكتاب والسنة والقياس وإجماع الأمة. ومن غريب ما وقع في هذه القضية إنكار بعض الجهلة من الحنفية علي في بسط هذا الكلام، بل أشار إلى أنه غير لائق بمقام الإمام. وهذا بعينه كما قال الضال جهم بن صفوان: وددت أن أحك مِن المصحف قوله تعالى: ثُمَّ اسْتَوَى عَلَى الْعَرْشِ ﴾ [الأعراف: ٥٤]، وإشارة الضال الآخر وهو أحمد بن أبي داود القاضي إلى الخليفة المأمون أن يكتب على ستر الكعبة: "ليس شيء وهو العزيز الحكيم." وقول الروافض الأكبر أنه بريء من المصحف الذي فيه نعت الصديق الأكبر. فإن لم تكن هذه لملا علي القاري فبها وإن كانت له فرجوعه عنها ثابت في "شرح الشفا" في الموضعين حيث قال: (وأما إسلام أبويه ففيه أقوال والأصح إسلامهما على ما اتفق عليه الأجلّة من السيوطي في رسائله الثلاث المؤلفة). (شرح الشفا" للقاري، ٦٠٥/١) وقال في مقام آخر: (وأما ما ذكروا من إحيائه عليه الصلاة والسلام أبويه فالأصح أنه وقع على ما عليه الجمهور الثقات، كما قال السيوطي في رسائله الثلاث المؤلفات). (شرح الشفا" للقاري، ٦٥١/١) ملحوظة : فرغ القاري من تأليف "شرح الشفا" سنة ١٠١١هـ، أي قبل وفاته بثلاث سنوات، فهو من آخر تاليفاته.
Know that in some copies of this commentary this statement was added: 'The parents of the Messenger of God (peace and blessings of God be upon him) died upon disbelief. This is a refutation of those who say that they died upon faith, or that they died upon disbelief then God revived them and they died in the station of faith. I have devoted an independent treatise to this issue and refuted what al-Suyūṭī mentioned in his three treatises supporting this claim with comprehensive proofs from the Book, Sunnah, analogy, and consensus of the community. Among the strange things in this matter is the denial of some ignorant Ḥanafīs against me in elaborating this speech, and they indicated that it is unbefitting the station of the imam. This is exactly like what the misguided Jahm b. Ṣafwān said: "I wish to erase from the Mushaf His statement: 'Then He established Himself over the Throne' (Qur'an 7:54)," and the signal of the other misguided one, Aḥmad b. Abī Dāwūd al-Qāḍī, to the Caliph al-Maʾmūn to write on the covering of the Kaʿba: "There is nothing, and He is the Mighty, the Wise." And the statement of the greatest Rāfiḍī that he is innocent of the Mushaf that contains the description of the greatest Ṣiddīq. If this is not from Mullā ʿAlī al-Qārī, then it is fine, but if it is from him, then his retraction from it is confirmed in 'Sharḥ al-Shifā', in two places, where he said: "As for the Islam of his parents, there are statements, the most correct is that they were Muslims, according to what the leading scholars agreed upon, as al-Suyūṭī [stated] in his three treatises." (Sharḥ al-Shifāʾ by al-Qārī, 1/605). And he said in another place: "As for what they mentioned of the Prophet (peace be upon him) reviving his parents, the most correct is that it occurred according to what the trustworthy majority hold, as al-Suyūṭī said in his three treatises." (Sharḥ al-Shifāʾ by al-Qārī, 1/651). Note: al-Qārī completed 'Sharḥ al-Shifā' in the year 1011 AH, i.e., three years before his death, so it is among his last works.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a note added in some copies of the commentary. It addresses the controversial topic of whether the parents of the Prophet Muhammad (ﷺ) died as believers or disbelievers. The author firmly states that they died upon disbelief (kufr). This is a refutation of those who claim they died upon faith, or that they were revived and then died as believers. The author claims to have written an independent treatise on the subject, refusing al-Suyūṭī's arguments in his three treatises. The passage then criticizes some ignorant Ḥanafīs who objected to the author's elaboration on this matter, comparing them to deviant figures: Jahm b. Ṣafwān, who wished to remove the verse about istiwāʾ from the Qur'an; Aḥmad b. Abī Dāwūd, who suggested writing 'There is nothing' on the Kaʿba covering; and the Rāfiḍī (Shīʿa) who disavows the standard Muṣḥaf containing praise of Abū Bakr. The author then notes that if these words are not from Mullā ʿAlī al-Qārī, it's fine; but if they are, his retraction is confirmed in his later work 'Sharḥ al-Shifā', where he states that the correct view is that the Prophet's parents were Muslims, following al-Suyūṭī and the majority of scholars. The note ends by observing that al-Qārī completed 'Sharḥ al-Shifā' in 1011 AH, three years before his death, making it one of his last compositions.

The Three Salaf Opinions on Testifying for Paradise
The author explains the three Salaf positions on testifying for paradise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three Salaf positions on testifying for a person's paradise and why certainty is limited to prophets.

Core Idea
Only prophets are guaranteed paradise; for others, we cannot be certain.

This passage addresses a delicate theological question: can we be sure that a particular person died upon faith and is destined for paradise? The Salaf were cautious, distinguishing between definitive, probabilistic, and community-based testimony. Understanding these positions helps us avoid overconfidence and respect the unseen nature of final salvation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the author's structure: first a clarification about the Prophet, then the general principle, then three opinions.
The passage begins with a variant reading about the Prophet.
2
Understand the Prophet's unique certainty
The author explains that the Prophet's death upon faith is certain and needs no proof. This sets the benchmark: only prophets have guaranteed paradise.
The Ten Promised Paradise themselves feared their end.
3
Identify the three opinions
Find the three stated opinions: (1) only prophets, definitive; (2) anyone with explicit scriptural evidence, probabilistic; (3) those for whom the community testifies, based on a hadith.
The first is cuti (definitive), the second zanni (probabilistic), the third ghalibi (predominant).
4
Reflect on the hadith and conclusion
The hadith shows that community testimony can indicate paradise or hell, but it is not definitive. The author calls it 'apparent and predominant'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي نسخة زيد قوله : ورسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم مات على الإيمان»، وليس هذا في أصل شارح تصدر لهذا الميدان لكونه ظاهرا في معرض البيان، ولا يحتاج إلى ذكره لعلوه صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم في هذا الشأن، ولعل مرام الإمام على تقدير صحة ورود هذا الكلام أنه صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم من حيث كونه نبيا من الأنبياء عليهم السلام وهم كلهم معصومون عن الكفر في الابتداء والانتهاء نعتقد أنه مات على الإيمان. وأما غيره من الأولياء والأصفياء بالأعيان فلا نجزم بموتهم على الإيمان وإن ظهر منهم خوارق العادات وكمال الحالات وجمال أنواع الطاعات، فإنّ مبنى أمره على العيان وهو مستور عن أفراد الإنسان، ولهذا كانت العشرة المبشرة وأمثالهم خائفين من انقلاب أحوالهم وسوء آمالهم في مآلهم. واعلم أن للسلف الله في الشهادة بالجنة ثلاثة أقوال أحدها أن لا يشهد لأحد إلا رحمهم للأنبياء عليهم السلام، وهذا ينقل عن محمد بن الحنفية والأوزاعي، وهذا أمر قطعي لا نزاع فيه. والثاني: أن يشهد لكل مؤمن جاء نص في حقه، وهذا قول كثير من العلماء لكنه حكم ظني. والثالث: أن يشهد أيضاً لمن شهد له المؤمنون كما في الصحيحين: ((أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام مر بجنازة فأثنوا عليها بخير فقال النبي صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم وجبت، ومرّ بأخرى فأثني عليها بشر فقال عليه الصلوة والسلام: وجبت. فقال عمر رضي الله تعالى عنه: يارسول الله ما وجبت؟ فقال رسول الله صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم: هذا أثنيتم عليه خيراً وجبت له الجنة، وهذا أثنيتم عليه شراً وجبت له النار ، أنتم شهداء الله في الأرض)) . وهذا أمر ظاهري غالبي، والله تعالى أعلم بالصواب.
And in the copy of Zayd, his statement: 'And the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, died upon faith.' This is not in the original of the commentator, who took up this field because it is apparent in the context of explanation, and there is no need to mention it due to his high rank, peace and blessings be upon him, in this matter. Perhaps the intent of the Imam, assuming the correctness of this wording, is that he, peace and blessings be upon him, in terms of being a prophet among the prophets, peace be upon them, and they are all protected from disbelief in the beginning and the end, we believe that he died upon faith. As for others, of the saints and the elite individuals, we do not judge with certainty their death upon faith, even if extraordinary acts, perfect states, and beautiful types of obedience appear from them, for the basis of the matter is on direct observation, and it is hidden from individual human beings. For this reason, the ten who were given glad tidings of paradise and the likes of them were afraid of the reversal of their states and the evil of their hopes in their final outcomes. And know that the Salaf, may Allah have mercy on them, have three statements regarding testifying for paradise: One of them is that one should not testify for anyone except, may Allah have mercy on them, the prophets, peace be upon them. This is narrated from Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyyah and al-Awza'i, and this is a definitive matter with no dispute. The second: That one may testify for every believer for whom a text has come in his favor, and this is the saying of many scholars, but it is a probabilistic ruling. The third: That one may also testify for the one for whom the believers testify, as in the two Sahihs: 'He, peace and blessings be upon him, passed by a funeral and they spoke well of her, so the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, said: "It is obligated." And he passed by another and they spoke ill of her, so he said: "It is obligated." So Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, said: "O Messenger of Allah, what is obligated?" The Messenger of Allah, peace and blessings be upon him, said: "This one you spoke well of, paradise is obligated for her, and this one you spoke ill of, hell is obligated for her. You are Allah's witnesses on earth."' And this is an apparent and predominant matter. And Allah most high knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage discusses the rule of testifying for a deceased person's final state of faith (iman) and specifically testifying that they are in paradise. It begins by addressing a variant reading in some copies concerning the Prophet's own death, clarifying that his death upon iman is certain and does not need mention. Then it moves to the general rule: for others, even saints and the Ten Promised Paradise, we cannot be certain of their final state. This leads to an enumeration of the three positions of the Salaf regarding testifying for paradise. The first position is definitive: only prophets. The second is probabilistic: anyone with explicit scriptural evidence. The third is based on the community's testimony, supported by a hadith where the Prophet affirmed paradise or hell based on people's praise or criticism. The passage concludes that the third is a general external matter, and Allah knows best.

العشرة المبشرة
خوارق العادات

ظن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That we can be certain of paradise for any righteous person, especially saints or those who performed miracles.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that even the Ten Promised Paradise feared their final outcome, and certainty is only for prophets.

Checkpoint

A) For all believers
B) Only for the prophets
C) For those whom the community praises

Key Takeaway
The Salaf only allowed definitive testimony of paradise for prophets; for others, it is speculative or based on community testimony, but never certain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The ten promised paradise were terrified of their end. If their faith and deeds did not grant them certainty, what makes you feel safe in your own salvation? What would it mean for you to live with that same fear and hope today?
?

?
The Prophet said you are Allah's witnesses on earth. If your community spoke of your life after your death, what would they say? Does your life align with the kind of testimony you would hope for?

Abu Talib Died a Disbeliever
Creedal statement on Abu Talib's disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the standard Sunni position on Abu Talib's death and the textual clarification regarding the phrase 'Abu Ali'.

Core Idea
Abu Talib, the Prophet's uncle, died outside of Islam—this is a settled creedal point.

This lesson addresses a specific historical figure: Abu Talib, the uncle of the Prophet Muhammad. The source passage from Fiqh al-Akbar states clearly that he died as a disbeliever. As we read, we will also note a textual nuance concerning the name 'Abu Ali' and why it likely refers to the same person.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text slowly. Notice the phrasing: 'وأبو طالب عمه مات كافرا'. Then read the commentary that follows.
The commentary expands on who 'عمه' refers to.
2
Identify the creedal claim
The passage makes a straightforward claim: Abu Talib died a disbeliever. Reflect on why this might be significant in Islamic theology.
This stance is part of Sunni orthodoxy.
3
Examine the textual issue
The commentary includes 'وأبو علي رضي الله عنه (مات كافرا)'. Consider the possible confusion: is 'أبو علي' a separate person or a scribal error?
Most scholars view this as a corruption; the intended referent is Abu Talib himself.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you grasped the core point.

Source Text

شرح الفقه الأكبر وأبو طالب عمه مات كافرا. وأبو طالب عمه أي عم النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وأبو علي رضي الله عنه (مات كافرا).
Commentary on Fiqh al-Akbar: And Abu Talib, his uncle, died a disbeliever. And Abu Talib, his uncle, that is the uncle of the Prophet, peace be upon him, and Abu Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, died a disbeliever.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage presents a creedal statement from Fiqh al-Akbar regarding Abu Talib's death as a disbeliever. The text begins with the title of the work, then quotes the original: 'وأبو طالب عمه مات كافرا'. The commentary clarifies that 'عمه' refers to the Prophet's uncle, and then adds 'وأبو علي رضي الله عنه (مات كافرا)'. The mention of 'أبو علي' is problematic and may be a textual corruption; the intended referent is likely Abu Talib himself. This passage affirms the standard Sunni position that Abu Talib died outside of Islam.

مات كافراً

What is stated about Abu Talib in this passage?
A) He died a believer.

D) He converted before death.

Key Takeaway
Abu Talib, the Prophet's uncle, died as a disbeliever according to Sunni creed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What does the fact that even the Prophet's own uncle died a disbeliever teach you about the absolute necessity of personal faith? Does your trust rest in your lineage, deeds, or affiliations? How will you ensure your own faith is genuine and not borrowed?
?
If you have a non-Muslim relative whom you love deeply, how does this lesson challenge any false comfort you may feel? What one step will you take this week to speak to them about the truth of Islam, knowing that death can come at any moment?

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate
5 min
The Subtle Distinction Between People of the Book and Polytheists
Author explains that despite rational judgment equating them, Jews and Christians are distinct due to Quranic texts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic text overrides the rational judgment that would otherwise classify Jews and Christians as polytheists, and why the Sabians remain subject to the rational ruling.

Core Idea
The Quran distinguishes Jews and Christians from polytheists, even though reason alone would not.

In this passage, Imam Ahmed Raza Khan Barelvi addresses a subtle point of jurisprudence: the relationship between rational judgment and revealed text. While reason tells us that anyone worshipping other than Allah is a polytheist, the Quran itself calls Jews and Christians 'People of the Book' and treats them distinctly from polytheists. This forces us to set aside the rational judgment for them, but not for others like the Sabians. Let's dive into the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the rational judgment
Read the first part of the passage where the author states the rational judgment: anyone worshipping other than Allah is a polytheist, even if they believe in books and prophets.
This is the default ruling that reason gives.
2
Identify the exception for Jews and Christians
Notice how the author says we contravene the rational judgment because of the Quranic text. The Quran describes their bad beliefs yet calls them 'People of the Book' and separates them from polytheists.
The text is the decisive factor.
3
Contrast with the Sabians
Read the final part: for the Sabians, no such textual distinction exists, so they remain under the rational judgment. We cannot analogize them to Jews and Christians.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلت وبالله التوفيق: هاهنا فرق دقيق هو أن قضية العقل هي المباينة القطعية بين الكتابية وعبادة غير الله سبحانه وتعالى فإنها هي الشرك حقاً والكتابي غير مشرك عند الشرع فكل من رأيناه يعبد غير الحقِّ جَلّ وعلا حكمنا عليه أنه مشرك قطعاً وإن كان يُقرّ بكتب وأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام، ولكنّا خالفناه هذه القضية في اليهود والنصارى بحكم النص، فإنّا وجدنا القرآن العظيم يحكي عنهم ما يحكي من العقائد الخبيثة ثم يحكم عليهم بأنهم أهل الكتاب ويُميزهم عن المشركين فوجب التسليم لورود النص بخلاف الصابئة؛ إذ لم يرد فيهم مثل ذلك فلم يجز قياسهم على هؤلاء ولا الخروج عن قضية العقل في بابهم. (الفتاوى الرضوية، ١٢٠/١٤)
I say, and with Allah is the success: Here is a subtle difference, and that is that the rational judgment is the absolute distinction between the Book-based religion and the worship of other than Allah, Exalted and High; for it (the latter) is truly polytheism, while the Book-based person is not a polytheist according to the Law. So everyone we see worshipping other than the Truth, Mighty and Exalted, we judge him to be a polytheist absolutely, even if he affirms books and prophets, peace be upon them. But we have contravened this judgment in the case of the Jews and Christians by virtue of the text (al-nass). For we find the Mighty Quran narrates about them what it narrates of evil beliefs, then judges them as People of the Book and distinguishes them from the polytheists. Therefore submission became obligatory due to the coming of the text. Contrary to the Sabians, for no such thing has come regarding them, so it is not permissible to analogize them to these (Jews and Christians), nor to depart from the rational judgment in their case. (al-Fatawa al-Radawiyya, 120/14)
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage discusses a subtle juristic-theological point: the rational judgment (qadiyyat al-'aql) draws an absolute distinction between the people of a revealed book and those who worship other than Allah. According to reason, anyone who worships other than Allah is a polytheist (mushrik) without exception, even if they affirm the prophets and books. However, the author says that we have set aside this rational judgment for the Jews and Christians because of the textual evidence (al-nass) from the Quran. The Quran narrates their evil beliefs yet calls them 'People of the Book' and distinguishes them from the polytheists. So we are obligated to submit to the text and not apply the rational ruling to them. On the other hand, for the Sabians, no such textual distinction came, so they remain subject to the rational judgment; we cannot analogize them to Jews and Christians. The author cites this from 'al-Fatawa al-Radawiyya'.

Key Terms
قضية العقل
الكتابي
المشرك
النص
الصابئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that Jews and Christians are polytheists because of their corrupt beliefs, based on rational reasoning.
Correction: The Quran itself distinguishes them as People of the Book and separates them from polytheists, so we must follow the text over reason.

Checkpoint
Why are Jews and Christians not treated as polytheists despite their corrupt beliefs?
A) Because their beliefs are not actually corrupt
B) Because the Quran distinguishes them as People of the Book
C) Because reason says so
D) Because of the consensus of the Companions

Key Takeaway
Jews and Christians are not treated as polytheists in Islamic law due to explicit Quranic texts, despite rational judgment to the contrary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found a command of Allah difficult to accept because it didn't seem rational to you? In light of this lesson, what would it mean for you to submit to the text even when your mind objects? What specific step can you take this week to practice that submission?
?
This lesson teaches that explicit revelation overrides personal reasoning. Is there an area of your life where you are following your own logic rather than what Allah has clearly commanded? What would change if you fully embraced the text as your guide in that area?
?
The author distinguishes between Jews/Christians and polytheists based solely on Quranic texts, not personal preference. Are there religious matters where you rely on your own opinion over what the Quran says? How can you train yourself to prioritize revelation over your own thinking?
